THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, JUNE 8, 1898.
WAS AT THE
BOMBARDMENT
San Juan Engagement
fc*y a Participant.
A YOUNGSTER'S DESCRIPTION
ONE SHELL WHICH CAME NEAR
THE FLAGSHIP.
Frank A. Kinne, a San Franciscan,
Gives a Graphic Account of the
Second Important Naval
Battle.
Frank A. Kinne, a San Francisco
youngster, graduate of the High School
here, and the son of Colonel C. Mason
Kinne, is acting second lieutenant of ma
rines aboard Admiral Sampson's flagship
New York. His description of the bom
bardment of San Juan, Porto Rico, writ
ten to his father, ia a vivid account of
the engagement of the Spanish batteries.
The Call is permitted to make these ex
tracts from the letter:
U. S. S. New York. Key West, Fla., May 19,
We arrived here last evening from San Juan
de Porto Rico, having been gone since May 4.
When we came over from Cuba hurriedly on
May 1 and began coaling ship, taking on
stores and ammunition for a long crui=e, most
of us thought that when we left we would
have some fighting to do and It proved true,
WAS ON THE FLAGSHIP
Acting LIEUTENANT PRANK 6- KINNE of the Marines, flag
ship New York, U. S. N.
although we did not meet the Spanish fleet,
we leit here at daylight on the morning of
May 4 and proceeded to the Cuban coast and
picked up the battleships lowa and Indiana,
monitors Terror and Amphitrite, cruisers
Montgomery and Detroit, tug Wampatuck.
collier Niagara and torpedo boat Porter. With
this rather formidable fleet we steamed east
ward, and our conjectures proved true that
we were to proceed to Porto Kico and tackle
the Spanish fleet if we could find them there.
We steamed slowly, as the speed of a squad
ron is the speed of the slowest ship, and the
monitorÃ are not very fast. However, we ar
rived about thirty miles north of San Juan
at midnight, May 8, and the admiral issued
final instructions, although several plans of
battle had been agreed upon before. Either to
find the Spanish fleet outside the harbor, or to
find them inside or not finding them at all. to
attack the fortifications. This last Mas what
we did as the enemy had withdrawn from that
locality the day previous, we Steamed very
slowly toward the harbor, feeling our way
and keeping a very sharp lookout for the
enemy.
At 3 a. m. all hands were called and at 3:30
a. m. a light breakfast was partaken of. At
daylight we arrived in the offing of the harbor,
but not a sign of the fleet. Signals were run
up from the flagship and we formed "line of
column" on the Iowa: next came the Indiana,
then the New York, Terror and Amphitrite. in
order named. The Montgomery. Detroit and
Wampatuck were sent out as flank scouts and
to mark the ten fathom line with stake boats
for turning point. The little Porter was sent
in to reconnolter and the Niagara with the
Wampatuck were to recede out of range offlre.
The attacking line steamed in a triangle, thus
formed by the stake boats, and each In turn
as It came in range poured forth a steady
stream of shells that created chaos in the
forts and town proper.
At 6:15 a. m. the signal gun for battle was
fired from the lowa, to be Instantly followed
by her great guns and 4-inch battery. The
Indiana opened fire with her 13-inch guns, fol
lowed by her 8-Inch and 10-inch. Then the
New York and the monitors. The cruisers ot>
either flank had come to a standstill and were
pouring broadside after broadside into the forts
and town. When our shells struck the forti
fications It seemed an if a volcano had Just
broke forth, with the rocks, dirt, dust, smoke,
etc. going up into mid air. The guns on the
Morro and water batteries were silenced In
stantly and the Morro completely demolished
One of the 13-Inch shells from the Indiana
cut a steeple of a church In two as it one
were cutting butter with a knife. This was
only our first round at them. They returned
our fire, of course, but did not get our range,
and most of the shots whizzed over us but
dropped rather close for all that, and 'were
not at all inviting. We could watch them in
the air Just before they struck and they looked
like little bees buzzing alonÃ, but I am very
much inclined to believe that their "sting"
would be of a far different nature than that of
the busy bee.
The second round was even more fierce than
the first, as the enemy had seemed to have
gotten our range better and their shells came
too confoundedly close for comfort at all but
still none of our ships were hit until we were
nearly out of range, when two of their shots
told on us.
We were drawing away from them, having
silenced and demolished all but three gnny
when a' shell struck a stanchion on our port
quarter. It exploded, the pieces flying in hll
conceivable directions, through smokestacks,
ventilators and decks and wrecking one of our
cutters, which we were compelled to throw
overboard. It killed one of the gun's crew In
stantly, striking him in the back of the head,
and wou,ndlng three others. We carried the
poor fellows below and made them us com
fortable as circumstances allowed. The lema.
was also struck once, wounding two men, and
these were all the casualties that occurred in
our fleet of seven vessels and nearly 2000 men
engaged. On the enemy's side the slaughter
must have been terrific, us all our shots told
and thirteen-inch shells exploding in their
midst are not anything to play with.
In watching the flight of one of their shells,
which was tumbling and raising quite a whir,
it reminded me of a duck being shot on the
wing and tumbling to the water below, so
realistic was the sight. For my part, I don't
like those kind of "ducks" around us.
Another amusing Incident of the day, If such
things In such times could be called amusing,
was the daring of that little spitfire of a tor
pedo boat Porter. While reennnoitering close In
shore she drew the fire of some riflemen upon
her. "What did she do but turn her stern to
the enemy and commenced firing her little one
pounder Into their mfdst; of course amid the
roar of the great guns her fire could not be
heard, but the continual and steady puffs of
smoke from her looked very saucy. It was
comical, indeed.
Having withdrawn to a safe distance, we
counted noses, as it were, and compared notes,
and found that we were all all right save the
few sad casualties mentioned above. At 5 p. m.
we stopped and half-masted our colors, for we
on the New York had that saddest of sad rites
to perform to one of our comrades who was
with us but a few hours before, and now cold
in death and to be buried at sea. The word
was passed, "All hands bury the dead," and
if anything makes me feel creepy and goose
fleshy it is that. We all laid aft and the chap.
laln performed the ritual service. The marine
guard presented arms and he that was was de
llvpred to the depths of the sea, "Taps"'
flights out) was sounded and then three stirring
volleys given to the brave fellow who died for
his country at his post of duty. We then pro
ceeded on for this place for coal and stores
and further orders.
Take It all through, the men were cool and
collected and showed the American spirit. Such
was our second fight. FRANK A- KINNE.
PROGRESSIVE MOVEMENTS.
Merchants' Association Fighting for
Cleaner Streets and Better
Lights.
The first meeting of the new board of
directors of the Merchants' Association
was held yesterday, and the following di
rectors were present: A. S. Baldwin,
Wallace Bradford, Joseph Brltton, Charles
Bundschu, Joseph D. Grant, Marshall
Hale, Hugo D. Kell, D. V. Kelly. George
Llebes, John E. Qulnn, Hugo Rothschild,
W. R. Sherwood and Rolla V. Watt. The
only absent directors were F. W. Dohr
mtinn, now In Europe, and Vanderlynn
Stow, now In the East. The following
officers were elected for the ensuing
year: President. F. W.Dohrmann; first vice
president. A. S. Baldwin; treasurer. V.
Stow, and second vice president, Hugo
D. Keil. Acting President Baldwin an
nounced the appointment of the following
standing committees: Public affairsâDi
rectors Kelly, Hale, Grant, Quinn and
Sherwood; publicity and promotionâDi
rectors Bundsphu, Watt, Bradford, Keil
1 and Llebes: trade and finance â Directors
i Britton, Rothschild, Stow, Bradford and
i Sherwood.
The following parties were elected as
| regular members of the association: M.
I C. Baker & Son, Paul P. liernhardt ->fc Co.,
Christian Froellch, Pacific Coast Syrup
Company and John Yennekohl. The
Board of Supervisors having adopted the
plans of the association for lighting the
streets of the city, it was decided to of
fer a reward of $20 for beat design of an
electric light pole to be erected along
Market street and Van Ness avenue.
The committee upon sprinkling the
streets with salt water reported progress.
Assayer Thomas Price has been employed
to make a report to the board upon the
effect of salt water upon bituminous
pavements. As soon as this report is re
ceived further action will be taken.
Regarding the abolition of poolrooms
within the city limits, the following com
munication was received from Chief of
Police Lees:
"In reference to your letters relative to
poolrooms where pools are sold on horse
races in this city, I Inclose reports from
Captains Wittman and SpiUane of May
20 and Juno 4, 1898. Their reports of June
4 show that no poolrooms selling pools
on horse racing exist in this city, but are
now situated at Oceanside, San Mateo
County, and Sausalito, Marln County,
where they have licenses and are Belling
pools on Eastern races."
As the efforts of the board had been
exerted toward this end, the report of the
Chief of Police was considered aa a very
favorable showing of its work. The Mer
chants' Association of New York reported
that the efforts made to prevent any re
duction in the free delivery system of the
malls had met with success. Answers
were received showing that our delegation
In Congress had favorably acted upon the
telegraphic request forwarded by the
Merchants' Association of San Francisco.
DR. OPHUELS APPOINTED.
Chair of Bacteriology at Cooper's Is
Filled.
Professor Ophuels has been appointed
to the chair of pathology and bacteriol
ogy at the Cooper Medical College. Dr.
Ophuels is a graduate of Gottingen Uni
versity, and for a time was an assistant
in that college. He comes to Cooper Col
lege with high recommendations from
Johns Hopkins University.
Dr. Abfams will confine his attentions
to clinical diagnosis, being relieved by Dr.
Ophuels.
Duelists Arraigned.
John Joseph Sehl and Charles Hammer
smith were arraigned before Judge Mo
gan yesterday morning on the charge ct
assault with a deadly weapon. Their at
torneys said they were not ready to pro
ceed, and by consent a continuance was
granted till Friday.
No complaints have as yet been sworn
to by either party, and It Is the general
belief that each will refuse to prosecute
the other and the cases will fall through
The police have been unable to find any
witnesses who will say that they saw the
shots exchanged between the parties In
the lodging house, 242 Post street.
The Capron Case.
United States District Judge Morrow
yesterday dismissed the suit brought
against Kearney and others of the Cap
ron heirs by Attorney General Fitzgerald.
The action was brought to prevent the
distribution of the Capron estate.
THEY ARE NO
MORE FRIENDS
John F. English at War
With Mrs. McCord.
HAD TROUBLE OVER WINDOWS.
A HIGH BOARD FENCE NOW DI
VIDES THEIR PROPERTY.
Both Parties Believing That They
Are Right, Befuse to Yield
an Inch in the
Matter.
A thirty-foot hieh fence now divides
the friendly feeling that once existed
between John F. English, a commis
sion merchant, and the widow of John
i McCord of the well known hay and
grain firm of Scott & McCord. The
present unpleasantness is due to a dis
: pute over property rights. The result
I of the row Is that several carpenters
, have been given employment, and the
i property owned by the disputants has
' been made unsightly by the high board
1 fence being erected under the direc
| tion of English.
Some three years ago English pur
chased a piece of property on Fell
street, between Central and Masonic
avenues, and erected a three-story
dwelling on it. At that time the prop
erty on the north side was not built
upon, and English took advantage of
that fact to get all the sunshine possi
ble in the house. The north side of the
house was studded with windows look
ing out upon the vacant lot, and every
thing was satisfactory until a few
weeks ago, when Mrs. McCord bought
the adjoining property. Acting under
the advice of her friends she decided
to erect three flats on the land. She
intends occupying the lower flat and
renting the two upper flats to increase
her Income and lighten her burden in
looking after her family.
It was undoubtedly due to an over
sight on the part of the architect that
all of the windows on the side of the
flat next to the house owned by Eng
lish are on a level and directly opposite
the windows of the English dwelling and
a three-foot passageway running along
the north side of the English property
is all that divides the houses. It would
not require an acrobat to pass from one
house to the other by means of the win
dows.
English was displeased when Mrs.
McCord began to build. Her house cut
off the morning sun from his house and
darkened the rooms. He could not
make any objection to the improving of
the property, however, but he did ob
ject when he found that nearly all the
windows on the south side of Mrs. Mc
â  Cord's building were placed directly
I opposite the window's on the north side
i of his house.
English and J. V. Hull, a carpenter
and builder, had a consultation with
the representatives of Mrs. McCord.
While they objected to the location of
the windows they said that they would
not' make complaint if stained glass,
stationary windows were put In the
McCord building. The McCord people
agreed to the stained glass windows,
but would not consent to stationary
windows. They represented that the
only way of ventilating the rooms on
that side of the house would be by
movable windows.
Then English directed Builder Hull
to erect a 30-foot fence to cut off the
view into his premises..
The light has been cut off on the
south side of the McCord building, and
while the fence remains there much
difficulty will be found in securing
tenants.
There is a possibility that the Mc-
Cord people will finally consent to the
stationary stained glass windows, and
then the high fence will come down.
ARRIVAL OF THE BELGIC.
The Crew and Steerage Passengers
Were Put in Quarantine.
The steamer Belgic arrived on time
from the Orient yesterday. A case of sus
pected smallpox was discovered in the
Chinese steerage during the voyage, and
the Mongol was put ashore at Honolulu.
Dr. RÂ«senau, federal quarantine officer,
ordered all the steerage passengers
ashore at the quarantine station and al
lowed the steamer to dock. After the
crew had been mixing with the people
from the shore for about half an hour
and the cabin boys had been on the dock
with some of the cabin passengers' bag
gnge, word came to send them all to the
island. In consequence every Chinese and
Japanese aboard was bundled into a tug
and sent into quarantine, where they will
be kept a fortnight. When the Belgtc left
Hongkong, for the week ending May 10
there had been 24G cases of bubonic plague
reported in that city and 220 deaths. Con
trary to all precedent the plague was rag
ing among the white residents.
The Belgic brought over twelve Euro
peans, thirty-five Japanese, two Mexicans
and 220 Chinese in the steerage, and the
following cabin passengers:

THE CHARTS WERE
VERY INCORRECT.
CAUSE OF THE WBECK OF THE
CORONA ASCERTAINED.
Seven Fathoms of Water in the Place
Where She Struck on the Beef
and Sank.
Captain O. F. Bolles and J. K. Bulgrer,
local Inspectors of steam vessels, have
completed their reports as to the loss of
the steamship Corona and the accident
on the Pomona.
In the first-mentioned case the board
found as follows: The Corona, Captain
George H. Pierce master, left Seattle Jan
uary 2, with freight and passengers for
Sitka and way ports. At 7:12 a. m. on
January 23 she struck on a reef near the
southeast end of Lewis Island, Arthur
Passage, British Columbia. She filled
with water In a short time and sank.
At the time of the accident the weather
was dark and cloudy. Acting Pilot H. C.
Kaufman steered the vessel in the same
way as is customary in that locality. At
the place where the ship struck there is
marked on the chart seven fathoms of
water and no indications of rocks. Cap
tain Pierce had made but one voyage pre
viously as master in these waters. The
board found that the actual damage to
the Corona, which was afterward raised,
was 130,000. The report concludes as fol
lows:
"After careful consideration of the case
we are of the opinion that Captain Pierce
was not to blame In connection with the
accident."
In the matter of an accident on board
the Pomona, being the blowing out of a
blind tube, near San Diego, causing the
death of Coal Passer Robert McNelu, the
board said;
"In our opinion the'explosion would not
have occurred had the eight tubes in the
starboard boiler not been removed and
the holes in the tube sheets closed with
blind tubes about twelve inches long, and
which had not been expended inside suf
ficiently to bear the frictlonal pressure on
the blind tube that blew out."
LIQUOR MEN ARE
UP IN ARMS
OBJECT TO SECTIONS OP THE
EVANS BILL.
A Strong Protest Sent to Washington
Against Crippling the Small
Dealers.
The announcement yesterday that the
measure popularly known as the Evans
bill had passed the House In "Washing
ton raised Into a breeze among local li
quor men. The bill provides that all
packages of one gallon and upward must
be stamped by the Revenue Department,
whereas up to now packages of less than
five gallons have been exempt.
The following protest, signed by forty
four prominent liquor firms, wu last
night sent to Washington:
We, the undersigned merchants of San Fran
cisco, object strongly to sections 4, 5 6, 7 and 8
of the Evans bill reducing the bonded period
and requiring Internal revenue inspection to
be made and stamps issued on packages less
than five gallons and not less than one gallon.
Our objections to these sections are that they
will not increase the internal revenue receipts
one dollar and that they afford no additional
protection against fraud. It will greatly de
crease the income of the department by adding
to the expenses as a larger force of Inspectors
must be employed to make the Inspection and
collect stamps.
To us the bill Is objectionable as It hampers
us In our business, delays us In the delivery
of "our goods sold. Increases our expenses, en
larges the details of our revenue work, sub
jects us to further espionage by revenue offi
cers without reason and without any corre
sponding benefit to the department.
We ask you to place this matter before Sena
tor White and the Pacific Coast delegation and
ask for us that they unite in determined op
position to the said sections. The rest of the
bill Is unobjectionable.
The advocates of the bill. Ignoring the
extra trouble entailed on dealers and rev
enue officials, claim that the sections will
minimize adulteration and "moonshin
lng."

THE MONTEREY OFF FOR. MANILA
Sailir>g of tf)e Mon
itor ar>d the Col
lier Brutus.
Early Next Week; the Fleet
of Troopships Will Sail.
Paymaster in Camp.
Next Monday is the date now fixed
upon by the local war lords for the
embarkation of the second expedition
to the Philippines, and Tuesday as
the day of departure. These dates
may be shortened by twenty-four or
possibly forty-eight hours before the
end of the week, but the prevailing
opinion around the various general
headquarters is that the troops and
transports will not be ready for the
long voyage before that time.
As for the strength of the expedi
tion, nothing has developed to change
the plans already announced in The
Call. The steamers China, Zealandia
and Colon will comprise the fleet,
while one battalion each from the
Eighteenth and Twenty-third United
States Infantry, the First Colorado,
Tenth Pennsylvania, two batteries
of light artillery from Utah and a
detachment of the Signal Corps will
be the occupants of the transports.
Second Lieutenant Hooper of the
First Colorado was sent aboard the

six men each from his own regiment
and the Tenth Pennsylvania to guard

Detachments will be sent to the other

AT CAMP MERRITT.
Portion of the Volunteers Given
Two Months' Pay â Seventh's
New Quartermaster.
Two mule teama drove out to Camp
J^errltt yesterday with sufficient coin to
pay the officers and men of the Colorado,
Pennsylvania and Utah troops up to
June 30, In acordance with Instructions
from Washington to give all troops two
months' pay prior to embarking for Ma
nila. Paymasters Whipple and Kllbourne
accompanied the treasure, which was
guarded by a detachment of cavalry. The
great pile of gold and silver was a source
of considerable Interest to the Pennsyl
vanlans, the majority of whom had never
before seen so much "hard money" at
one time. The others, of course, coming
frem the West, were entirely familiar
with the "good red gold."
All the regiments have transmitted
their muster rolls to the pay Inspector,
and more coin will be disbursed during
the week.
Captain A. W. Klmball, promoted from
first lieutenant and quartermaster of the I
Seventh California, to captain and as- I
sistant quartermaster, has been relieved
from duty with that regiment and or
dered to report to General Merriam, com
manding the Department of California,
for duty. D. C. Williams, who for the
past four years has been connected with
the quartermaster's depot in this city,
under Major Long, has been appointed to
succeed Captain Kimball on Colonel
Berry's start. .Lieutenant Williams is a
native of Kentucky and a graduate of
the Military Academy of Ohio. His
brother, Colonel Charles W. Williams,
was formerly in charge of the supply de
pot here, but is now stationed at Jeffer
sonville.
Colonel Berry of the Seventh California
will detail three qtiicers to go to the
southern part of the State for recruits
as soon as orders come from Washington
to fill the regiment to the maximum of
106 to the company. It is Colonel Berry's
intention to rill each company with re
cruits from its home station, and he says
there will be no difficulty whatever In se
curing all the men he needs in two days'
time. The recruiting officers expect to
start on their mission the latter part of
the week.
The Twentieth Kansas Regiment and
one battalion from Idaho were Inspected
in heavy marching order by Colonel
Hughes of General Merritt's staff yester
day morning. In the afternoon the Sec
ond Idaho Battalion and the Wyoming
troops were inspected. The men marched
to the southern slope of the Presidio
reservation, where they pitched their
shelter tents and broke carifp under the
Inspector's supervision. The Seventh
California. First Montana and the two
Dakota regiments will be Inspected dur
ing the week.
A detachment of the Signal Corps has
gone into camp on the field with the
Fourteenth Infantry.
A brigade court-martial sat in the head
quarters of the Third Brigade yesterday
and tried several prisoners for minor of
fenses.
Majors Btrother, Sturgls and Hale re
ported to Major-General Otis yesterday
for duty. They are attached to his staff.
Captain William A. Tucker, commis
sary of subsistence of volunteers, has '
been ordered to report to General Otis
at Camp Merritt for duty as chief com
missary on the staff of General Ander
son, who sailed with the First Brigade
for Manila. Captain Tucker will sail with
the next brigade. -
Brigadier-General Green has established
his headquarters adjoining Major-General
Otis' headquarters. General. M. P. Miller
is located on the same lot with the en
gineer company.
CHRISTIAN COMMISSION.
Special Meeting To-Day of
Evangelical Clergymen
for Army Work.
Deep interest is manifested on the part
of the city clergy in regard to the United
Sfnfs Army Christian Commission of the
Young Men's Christian Association, which
has been organized in nearly all the
States and larger cities of the Union.
The work is to be conducted similar to
that done during the civil war, and as the
work of the Young Men's Christian Asso
ciation is exclusively a work for men by
men it hns been unanimously decided in
all the Eastern States that this is the
proper agency through which this line of
work should he undertaken; consequently
the War Department has given It its most
unqualified Indorsement. When the first
volunteers were called W. B. Millar, one
of the International Young Men's Chris
tum Association secretaries, was detailed
for this special work, and proceeded at
once to Washington. D. (\. where he
rÂ»-:i <iiiy received all necessary approval of
the work. General John MeCook of New
York la chairman of the national move
ment and D. L, Moody, General O O
Howard, Major Whittle and other promi
nent evangelists have charge of the evan
gelistic department.
The city clergymen, in consultation
Monday, decided this was the legitimate
course to pursue, and have called a meet-
Ing of the special committee to take place
at the Young Men's Christian Associa
tion building to-day at 2 o'clock^ when it
In fxpr-cted Mr. McCoy will outline the
course of procedure, and the United
States Army Christian Commission of the
Young Men's Christian Association will
probably be organized for the State of
California next Monday. It is confidently
hoped that D. L. Moody will be In San
Francisco in a few weeks to take up spe
cial evangelistic work at Camp Merritt
under direction of the San Francisco
Young Men's Christian Association.
THE ARTILLERY CAMP.
Five Batteries in the Field.
Fourth U. S. Cavalry
Awaiting Orders
The artillery ramp on the Presidio plain
engages the attention of spectators and
elicits comments of commendation from
officers of the army. Yesterday Captain
Birkhimer's Battery L, Third Artillery,
came over from Lime Point on the steam
er McDowell and joined Major Hess' camp
of artillerymen. The camp at present
contains three batteries of the Third
United States Artillery and two batteries
of California Volunteer Heavy Artillery.
Captain Hobbs' battery of the Third Ar
tillery, now at Alcatraz, may join the
camp to-day.
Recruiting for the artillery Is progress
ing In a manner highly gratifying to Ma
jor Hess. The maximum strength of a
battery of heavy artillery is two hundred
men. Should the present activity In re
cruiting be maintained for two weeks the
full force of twelve hundred for the six
batteries of the camp will be secured.
.Lime Point, which Major General Nel-
Bon A. Miles is pleased to term the Gib
raltar of the Pacific, is now garrisoned
by Battery B of California Volunteer Ar
tillery. The station is one of the most
Important on the Pacific Coast. When
but few troops were stationed here mili
tary men expressed the hope that The
Call would not direct attention to the fact
that but a small garrison was on duty at
i.ime Point. It was said that the Span
THE SA^ FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, JinSTE 8, 1398.
lards might land a small force on the
Marin coast, seize the battery of high
power guns and practically demolish the
forts on this side of the channel. The
Lime Point battery is one of the highest
in the world. A trained force occupying
the works could destroy the batteries on
this side of the Golden Gate.
The news published in The Call a few
days ago that the Fourth United States
Cavalry had been assigned to the First
Brigade of the expeditionary forces des
tined for the Philippines gave rise to a
wild story that the regiment had received
orders to embark for Manila. The troop
ers have received no orders other than
those published in The Call, and they do
not expect to be included among the
forces to sail next week, although orders
to go at once would be welcome news.
The following order was issued yester
day:
PRESIDIO OF SAN FRANCISCO,
June 7, 1898.
Each troop commander will submit without
delay the name of sergeant fitted by lengrth of
service and soldierly qualities to be a member
of the Gnnrd of the Standard. By order of
LIEUTENANT-COLONEL MORRIS.
GEORGE H. CAMERON, First Lieutenant
Adjutant.
Seven troops of the regiment are now
at the Presidio, and another troop will be
along In a day or two. The seven organi
zations now here have been recruited to
the maximum of 100 for each troop.
Among the recruits from the East were
many fine youngsters, who came with let
ters commending them to the officers of
the Fourth. The standard Is high in this
organization of regular cavalry. College
graduates, robust young fellows of means
and others who prefer the life of a troop
er in time of action to a leisurely exist
ence in a comfortable Eastern home may
be found in the regiment.
VOLUNTEER SIGNAL CORPS
Expert Telegraphers Wanted,
to Whom L-iberal Pay
Will ]Be Given.
There is a demand for expert telegraph
operators in the United States Volunteer
Signal Corps, the recruiting office for
which is on the fourth floor of the Phelan
building. The term of enlistment Is for
the war. Any operator desiring to enlist
must present a certificate from a man
ager of the Western Union, the Postal or
some other well-recognized telegraph or
ganization, that he is a competent tele
grapher, of high moral character, correct
habits and of such standing that the man-
agcr or superintendent signing the certi
ficate would be willing to employ him as
an operator in his own oflice. There
seems to be a lack here of such men de
sirous of enlisting in the Signal Corps,
and hence recruits will be sent for from
the Eust.
The quota of electricians and other men
is now full, and all applicants for enlist
ment for this branch of the service are
being declined.
Under the law the Signal Corps Is au
thorized to consist of one colonel, one
lieutenant-colonel, one major as disburs
ing orncer, and such other officers and
men as may be required, not exceeding
one major for each army corps, and two
tains, two first lieutenants, two see
on", lieutenants. live tlrst-claes ser
vants, ten sergeants, eleven corporals
and thirty first-class privates to each or
ganized division of troops, provided that
two-thirds of all officers below the rank
of major and a like proj^rtion of the en
listed men shall be skilled electricians or
telegraph operators. Something over 100
men according to this arrangement, are
to be enlisted in the Signal Corps, now
being organized for the Philippines. The
"'"of Signal Corps men is higher than
that Â°n other branches of the military ser
vice, except in the Engineer Corps, where
it is the same, it being as follows: First
class sergeants. $:,4 per month; sergeants.
$4080- corporals, $24; privates, $20 41).

Supplied With Arms.
Guns and ammunition were supplied to
the two battalions of Washington Volun
teers at Fontana Barracks yesterday. The
guns are Springfield rifles of the latest
pattern. The non-commissioned officers
have been furnished with revolvers, and
each company has two shotguns, which
are to bo used for the killing of game.
This nearly completes the equipment of
the Washlrigtonians, who are now anxious
to try their guns on the Spaniards as tar
gets.
Yesterday a party of ladles presented
to the Washington Volunteers a flag of
the regulation size, and it was at once un
furled to the breeze from the flagstaff of
the Fontana Barracks.
Washington Volunteers.
Private Howard D. Madison of Company
E, First Washington Volunteer Infantry,
found guilty by a general court-martial
at Fontana Barracks of willfully disobey
ing the orders of his superior officer, and
sentenced to be dishonorably discharged
and to be confined at hard labor for one
month, with the forfeiture of all his pay,
will, by command of General Merriam, be
imprisoned at the Presidio.
Private John \V. Stephens of Company
A, First Washington Volunteer Infantry,
convicted by a general court-martial of
being absent without leave and using pro
fane and abusive language to a non-com
missioned officer in the execution of his
duty, has been sentenced to be confined at
hard labor in charge of the guard for one
month, and to forfeit $12 of his pay. By
command of General Merriam the sen
tence will be executed at the place where
his company may be serving.
LOVE'S LABOR.
A Father Crosses the Continent
to Say Good- By to His
Son.
There was a scene of parental and filial
affection at the Occidental Hotel last
night when the overland influx arrived.
W. H. Saam of Irwin, Pa., one of the ar
rivals, jumped down from the bus and
dashed into the lobby, where he was
greeted by a young soldier of the Penn
sylvania Regiment. The young man was
no other than his son Kennett, and the
purpose of his father's visit was a com
plete surprise to him.
The elder Saam says his trip to Califor
nia is a complete surprise to himself. He
was returning home to his lunrh ,mo day
last week when a friend informed him
that the ship on which his .sou uuuid muj
would leave this city in six days. He did
not hesitate a moment, but, ordering his
baggage to be packed at once, he started
for California a few hours later. Mis only
object was to grasp his son's hand once
more before he leaves for th<? Philippines
and to wish him godspeed and a bon voy
age.
Father and son walked up and down the
corridor of the hotel with arms locked
and it was a late hour when they sepa
rated for the night with a host of plans
formed for the morrow.
Reception to the Swedes.
The Swedish soldiers from the camps of
volunteers were given a reception in the
Swedish Congregational Church last even-
Ing. It was not only a reception, but also
a banquet in a small way, ami all that
was not consumed by the visitors was
packed up and will be takon to the
camps this morning. Re\ . C;. rl Anderson
of the Swedish church of Oakland and
Rev. Albin Anderson, pastor of the local
church, were the speakers of the evening.
In the Swedish language they exhorted
their hearers to be true to their traditions
of their race and to the flag in whose ser
vice they were enlisted, and they called
up the familiar names In Swedish and
Scandinavian history and cited them as
examples to be remembered and fol
lowed.
There was singing by tho Sunday-school
children and by the oholr. and then every
one was invited Into the basement of the
church, where tho ladies had spread a
supper Mt to attr.-.ct any number of hun
gry volunteers and to provide for them
all. There were Dearly a hundred soldiers
and as many more visitors at the church
and the affair was voted a grand success'

A YELLOW WAIL.
One Poor Special Commis
sioner of the Examiner Be
moans The Call's
Enterprise.
Through the medium of a carrier pigeon this wall has reached the
joss house across the streetâ also the public. It Is a message from a
special commissioner of yellow Journalism chiding his superiors for
their rank stupidity and Informing them that, as usual, in a matter
that would naturally present itself to any mind of ordinary intelligence,
they wore found wanting.
Undeterred by the Jarring he had received at the hands of ex-
Presldent Cleveland but a short time before, Chief Faker Hearst, upon
learning of the great victory at Manila through the Herald-Call dis
patches, set about to use the hero of the battle as an advertisement for
his illegitimate schemes. Dewey was on the other side of the globe,
and if he did object to being made a mark for lurid journals the ob
ject would be accomplished before his protest could reach this country.
So a tawdry admiral's ensign was secured and given in charge of a
"special commissioner" to present to Dewey. The commissioner and his
prize package sailed on the Charleston, which left for the Philippines
on May 22. On June 6 a carrier pigeon was shot near Salinas, and it
was found to bear the following message from the "commissioner":
ON BOARD U. S. CHARLESTON. Noon, Sunday, May 22.â Examiner,
San Francisco: We are now well on our way steaming at about twelve
knots. The weather Is beautiful and there Is a steady swell on. Many of
the Jackles are more or less affected, but If It gets no worse to-morrow
will see them all with good sea legs on. The Farallones are just to the
northeast. Here is a message from Captain Glass to bulletin and publish if
you see fit:
ON BOARD U. S. CHARLESTON. AT SEA. Noon, May 22. -Editor
Examiner: Please ask Navy Pay Office to report us Lat. 37-23 N. Long.
723-07 W. Weather fine. Light breeze from S. W. Sea moderate. Made
a fine start.
(Signed) GLASS.
PrivateâNo papers were sent to me this morning and The Call loaded the
ship with a large supply to be taken to the fleet and to Honolulu. For
C 's sake get me a supply of all papers off on the Peking. Will fly the
last birds at 3 p. m. Glass will fly some later to the Navy Yard, but I
don't think they will get there. DOUGLAS WHITE.
There was not enough legitimate enterprise among the entire lot of
Hearstllngs to send a bundle of papers out to the first ship that would
reach the fleet at Manila.
But the thought suggests itself that possibly the omission was Inten
tional. Naturally, the commissioner could not be expected to capture
the admiral the moment the Charleston dropped anchor off Cavite, and
the hero of Manila Bay might obtain a copy of the saffron sheet con
taining an intimation of what was in store for him, in which event he
would probably exchange the commissioner with the Spaniards for a
yellow dog and order the offering of a feather-brained editor thrown
overboard. If this surmise be correct, there Is no chance for Admiral
Dewey ,to escape the infliction.
But the world knows now that no Examiners went out on the
Charleston, and there Is the commissioner's admission that there were
plenty of Calls on board.

Sixth California Infantry.
The Sixth California Infantry Volun
teers may not be sent to Manila, but It
seems to be accepted that the regiment
Is to have active and important service.
It is understood now that Colonel John
son, with headquarters and Companies
B and G, will garrison Benicia barracks.
Companies C, D, E and H will probably
be sent to Fort Point to re-enforce the
artillery in charge of the high-power
guns defending this harbor. Majors
Grant and Whitman may command the
Infantry at Fort Point.
Colonel Johnson has detailed Lieuten
ants Walthall and Mexter as recruiting
officer to enlist men to fill existing com
panies to the maximum of 106 men each.
Recruiting stations will be established in
the San Joaquin Valley.

FOR THE INVALIDED.
WARD IN THE FIELD HOSPITAL.
The field hospital at Camp Merritt Is now thoroughly established,
and yesterday forty-three soldier patients were being cared for. Major
W. O. Owen, U. S. A., Is the surgeon in charge, with Major E. R. Mor
ris and First Lieutenant Henry Page as his assistants. Major Morris
has charge of the non-infectious cases, while Lieutenant Page is in con
trol of the infectious cases. Major Owen is the executive head.
Of the forty-three patients now in the hospital, thirty-one are
suffering from measles, and all but one are doing nicely. Half a dozen
of the cases under Major Morris' care were turned over to him by the
French Hospital yesterday, all of whom were convalescing from serious
ear complaint.
At one time measles threatened to become epidemic, but this dan
ger has been overcome, and the cases in the hospital are kept com
pletely isolated, nobody but the surgeons and attendants being al
lowed to approach within thirty yards of the ward.
One hundred or more enlisted men will be transferred from the Pre
sidio to the hospital, and those who are not needed to attend the in
valids will be thoroughly drilled for the more serious work in store for
them in the Philippines.
The hospital is completely equipped with instruments, and medi
cines, and everything is as neat and tidy as can be. The surgeons report
the troops at Camp Merritt in an exceedingly healthy state, consider
ing the changeable climate, which is most trying on the men from the
interior States.
MAY BE
A DOUBLE
MURDER
A Woman and Her
Brother Stabbed.
GUIDI'S INSENSATE FURY
MADDENED WITH LIQUOR, HE
RUNS AMUCK.
Possibly Fatal Termination of a Long
Domestic Quarrel â The Assail
ant Arrested and Lodged
in the Tanks.
Family trouble of long standing culmi
nated last night in Santtno Guidi stabbing
his wife and her brother, Londo Pelle
grini, and wounding them so severely that
the recovery of both Is doubtful. Guidl
was arrested and taken to the Central
Police Stntion and placed in the "tanks."
No charge will be preferred against him
until the result of the Injuries ca"n be
definitely ascertained. Pellegrini was
taken immediately to the Receiving Hos
pital, but Mrs. Guidi insisted upon being
removed to the home of her sister, where
she was attended by the family physician,
after which she was Transferred to the
hospital.
Mrs. Erida Guldi and her husband were
separated about three months ago on
account of his alleged cruelty, and since
then she has been living with MrS. Qui
cili, a married sister, whose home is at 636
Broadway. Londo Pellegrini visited his
sister at this place last evening and about
11:30 o'clock asked Erida and Lillie, a
younger sister, to get him some tamales.
They went to a coffee-house on Stockton
street, and after making their purchases
started for home, but when they reached
Broadway they saw Guidi and, fearing
him on account of threats he had made
before, turned back and went around the
block to avoid him. When they again
reached Broadway Lillie saw Guidi near
some steps in front of a stable at 630 and
warned her sister that they had better go
into a store and wait, but Erida thought
it better to make a run for the house
As they passed the steps Guidi made a
dash for his erstwhile wife, and Lillie saw
lhat he had a knife. Guidl, who was con
siderably the worse for liquor, succeeded
in catching the object of his fury and
plunged the knife into her body five
times. He then grabbed Lillie, but she
succeeded in tearing herself loose from
his grasp and ran to her home and in
formed her brother of the stabbing
Pellegrini immediately hastened to
avenge his sister and encountered the
now thoroughly maddened Guidi at the
foot of the steps of his married sister's
house The two men grappled and strug
g ed for some little time, out Guidi fin
ally got the better of his brother-in-law
and succeeded in stabbing him three
times. Officer Connor and O. H. Berge
the special policeman on Broadway were
attracted to the scene by the desperate
fight and captured Guidi before he had a
chance to escape.
The knife with which Guidi did the
stabbing is a dagger which has been made
by grinding down a common table knife
and has a blade about four inches long as
sharp as a razor. Mrs. Guidl has five
woundÃ, and the nature of them renders
her recovery very doubtful. One of the
stabs is In the left breast near the heart,
and there are two more in the left side
below the breast. The other wounds are
in the right side.
At the Receiving Hospital, where Pelle
grini's injuries were dressed, Dr. Fltzgib
bon found . that the weapon had pene
trated the lung, liver and abdominal cav
ity and his recovery is very doubtful.
Guldi, when arrested, was brought to the
hospital, where Pellegrini identified him
as his assailant. On his bed In the hos
pital, while racked with pain and almost
fainting, Pellegrini told his story, as fol
lows:
"Guidi has been out of work for some
time, and out of pity for mj sister I took
the two of them into my housso. After a
short time I found that as long as the
man could live off me and get drunk oc
casionally he did not care much about
finding work. This was bad enough, hut
when he took to coming home drunk and
beating his wife It was more than I
could stand, so I threw him out. Last
night Erlda and my other sister, Lillie,
went out on an errand.
"They were gone but a short time, when
Lillie name running back, screaming 'For
nake, come quick; Santlno is killing
Erida.' 1 rushed down tho strret, and
found the man bending nvor my si?t<r
who was covered with blood. I did* not no
tice the knif" at first, but as I approached
he made a vicious lunge at me with it.
SeHng ho was armed I ran to Captain
QuiciU's saloon on the corner. I stumbled
and fell on the threshold, and before I
could gft up Guidi had plunged the knife
into me."
Pellegrini made an ante-mortem state
ment to the police definitely accusing
Guidi of the stabbing.

ALL POSITIONS ARE
FILLED OR PROMISED
WASHINGTON, June 7.â A1l the po
rtions in the Volunteer Army under the
'PresidÂ°nt'B first rail have either been
filled or the plaros have been prom-
Is, d, and the remaining nominations to
them will soon be sent, to the Senate.
The department has not yet consid
ered the selection of the officers for the
organizations to be formed under the
Second rail fur 75,000 men.
Fully 50,000 of the 75,000 men will be
d to recruit thf existing organiza
tions to their full legal maximum
strength, which will leave only 25,000 to
be divided into organizations, the form
ing of which as such will not take place
for some time.
Civil War Disabilities Removed.
WASHINGTON, June 7.â The bill for
the removal of all political disabilities
arising from the Civil War is now a
law. President McKinley has formally
approved it. Representative Jenkins
of WisconHin is its author. The law
declares "that the disabilities imposed
by section 3, fourteenth amendment to
the conntltutlon, heretofore Incurred,
ax* hereby removed."

WORKING DAY AND NIGHT
ALERT OUT OF COMMISSION AND
DISMANTLED.
Corwin Takes Coal and Provisions
and Is Ready for Sea â Arrival
of the Grant and the
Whitgift.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
VALLEJO, June 7. â The United
States steamship Alert has gone out of
commission. She has been dismantled
and hauled out in ordinary alongside
her sister ship, the Ranger.
Passed Assistant Engineer Armin
Hartrauth of ti\e Mohican was de
tached prior to that vessel sailing for
Houolulu and assigned to the Brutus
as chief engineer. Passed Assistant
Surgeon Moore of the Alert has been
ordered to the Brutus.
The Philadelphia is still in the dry
' dock and work upon her is progressing
â¢ rapidly. She is receiving a coat of war
paint and a force of workmen is kept
busy night and day on her, but as
there is considerable yet to be done it
will be fully sixty days before she will
be ready to put to sea.
The Corwin has been fitting out at
the yard since Friday and has received
coal, water, stores and ammunition.
She is ready to sail, but her destina
tion is unknown.
The British steamer Whitgift of Lon
don, purchased by the Government to
carry a cargo of coal to Manila and
tuw the Monadnock to the Philippines
from Honolulu, arrived to-day and
made fast to the dock astern the moni
tor. Work on her machinery was be
gun at once and she will be docked and
painted the regulation war color.
The cutter Grant has arrived and is
taking on powder and stores for sea.
It is very likely she will be detailed to
act in conjunction with the Wheeling
in conveying treasure ships down the
coast as far as Puget Sound.
WAR REVENUE BILL
CONFEREES AT WORK
It Is Probable That a Substitute
Silver Provision Will Be
Reported.
WASHINGTON, June 7.â After sit
ting until past midnight last night the
conferees of the two houses on the war
revenue bill resumed their sitting
promptly at 10 o'clock to-day.
When the Conference Committee ad
! journed at noon Senator Allison cx
; pressed the opinion that it would be
able to report to the Senate and House
by to-morrow. He said that excellent
progress was being made, but that It
was impossible for the present to give
details of the proceedings. The House
members are manifesting a disposition
to accept the minor amendments with
out cavil, and the indications now are
j that at least 90 per cent of the Senate
! amendments will be retained without
, serious dispute. The more important
I amendments are naturally the ones
j upon which there is most controversy,
and so far the seigniorage provision has
proved to be the most difficult problem
with which the committee has had to
deal. The Republican members of the
conference are naturally opposed to
! any provision for coining the silver in
the treasury, but th^y realize that there
i will be great difficulty to get the Sen
; ate to accept a report that does not
make some provision of this character.
The probabilities now are that a substi
tute silver provision will be reported.

MRS. SARTORIS WILL
WED AN EX-CONFEDERATE
Daughter of General Grant to Cast
Her Fortunes With General
Douglass.
BAR HARBOR, Me.. June 7.â One of
the most notable weddings this year will
be tho marriage of Mrs. Nellie Grant-
Sartoris to General Henry K. Douglass
of Maryland. Mrs. U. S. Grant will come
to Sorrento this week and with her will
come Mrs. Sartoris.
The marriage of the daughter of Gen
eral Grant to General Douglass is another
of the many tokens of the reunion of the
North and South. General Douglass is
one of the old-time soldiers, and was a
prominent figure In Bar Harbor life in the
eighties. Ho is a very striking personage,
of decidedly military bearing, and is a
millionaire. General Douglass fought
against the T'nion in the civil war. He has
Just been appointed assistant adjutant
general In the army of the nation he once
sought to disrupt, and is now in the South
and expects to go to Cuba. He is the last
surviving member of Stonewall Jackson's
staff, and is one of the noted military men
of the last war.
Before Nellie Grant married Algernon
Sartoris, General Douglass was her ad
mirer, and the gallant Confederate soldier
has been very attentive since last sum
mer, when he was at Sorrento for a few
weeks, and visited the Grants. He will
come here later in the season, and it Is
said late In tho fall the daughter of Gen
eral Grant will preside over the Douglass

DRIFTING AIMLESSLY
ON THE WAR CURRENT
Senor Moret Admits That Spain's
Affairs Are Now in Complete
Disorder.
LONDON, June 7.â The Madrid cor
respondent of the Morning Post has
had an Interview with Senor Moret,
formerly Secretary of the Colonies, in
the course of which he said:
"The Government does not know
where it is going. Moreover, there is
not a person in Spain who can tell the
outcome of the present situation, which
is far more grave that is generally con
fessed. The Government has no fixed
plan. It allows matters to follow their
own course, heedless erf the ultimate
consequences.
"A campaign in favor of peace has
begun in the press and Is progressing
among the populace. I am convinced
that if a plebiscite were held peace
would be unanimously voted, even by
the soldiers. Parliament is divided as
well as the Ministers, who know not
whether to turn to the right or left.
The affairs of Spain are in complete
disorder."

TO PRESENT AMERICA
WITH A BATTLE-SHIP
HONOLULU, May 31.â The American
ship J. B. Walker, bound from Kobe to
New York, arrived off port on the 26th.
The captain came in to learn the latest
war news. After sizing up the situ
ation, he concluded that it was perfect
ly safe to continue his voyage around
the Horn. The American ship Kenil
worth, Cantain Baker, will leave Hilo,
Hawaii, to-morrow, bound for New
York with a valuable cargo of sugar.
Frank McStocker has advanced an
idea which has met with much favor
in this city. It is no less than a
scheme to assist in presenting the
United States with a battle-ship. The
plan suggested is for every American
and every sympathizer in the country
to give one day's salary to the fund.
When the details of the proposition are
settled upon there will be a ready re
sponse with funds.
Memorial day was fittingly observed
yesterday. Stores and Government of
fices closed at noon. The remainder
of the day was made a holiday. The
parade was made up of six companies
of volunteers, two companies of regu
lars, a detachment of blue jackets from
the Bennington, a detachment of the
Army and Navy Union and the mem
bers of George W. de Long Post. G. A.
R. t the only post outside of the United
States. Rev. J. A. Cruzan of Cali
fornia was the orator of the day.

EXPLANATION.
Th arrow flies with the wind. The top fig
ures ai station indicate minimum temperature
for the days; those underneath it. If any, the
amount o:' rainfall or of melted snow in Inches
and hundredths during the last twelve hours.
Ifinhars, or solid lines, connect points of equal
air pressure; isotherms, or dotted lines, equal
temperature. The word "high " means high
barometric pressure and Is usually accompanied
1 y :air weather: "low" refers to low pres
eure and is usually preceded and accompanied
by cloudy weather and rains. "Lows" usually
first appear on the Washington coast. When
the pressure is high in the Interior and low
al>ng the coast, and the isobars extend north
and south along the coast, rain is probable;
but when the "low" is inclosed with Isobars of
marked curvature, rain south of Oregon is Im
probable. With a "high" In the vicinity of
Idaho, and the pressure falling to the Cali
fornia coast, warmer weather mey be expected
In Fummer and colder weather In winter. The
reverse of these conditions will produce an
opposite result.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECASTS.
The pressure has remained nearly stationary
during the past twenty-four hours over the
country west of the Rocky Mountains. It is
relatively low throughout Eastern California,
Arizona and Nevada.
The temperature has fallen slowly over Cali
fornia and Oregon. It has risen over Idaho,
â Wyoming and Montana, The temperatures in
the great valleys of California are from 5 to
11 degrees above the normal.
The following maximum wind velocities are
reported: Carson City, 36 miles per hour from
the south; Point Reyes, 36. northwest.
No rain has fallen west of the Rocky Moun
tains.

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. June The stock market
showed the same conflicting tendencies as for
several days paHt. but the reactionary element
sained -the upper hand during the afternoon and
effected sharp declines all through the list, dis
lodging quite a large amount of stock, wiping
out the margins on which it was held. There
was a significant period of dullness succeeding
this lively Belling and then began a 'iulet and
steady absorption of stocks, offerings being
taken at the ruling prices, with an entire ab
sence of any effort to mark up quotations.
It was sufficiently evident that the purpose
of the reaction was. to secure stocks at the
lower level. There was quite a lively commis
sion house demand at the opening, which lifted
prices of many stocks a small fraction. There
was a special show of aggressive strength in
Brooklyn Transit, the Heading stocks, Erie
First preferred and some of the lower South
.westerns. Metropolitan Street Railway and
Consolidated Gas were also centers of strength.
The bears met this by vigorous attack on Union
Pacific preferred, People's Gas, Louisville and
Nashville and Kansas and Texas preferred, all
of which were driven down between IV. and 2
points. This combination proved vulnerable to
attack and weakened the market at Its salient
points. . The recoveries In the final hour were
over a point In a number of Important cases
and net changes were mostly small and losses
predominated. The war news was without ln-
Jluence on the market. London was quite a
heavy seller here and was a factor In weak
ening prices and in giving a firm tone to ster
ling exchange. The demoralization of wheat
prices may have aided ' the reaction The
world's heavy receipts of wheat gave rise to a
question as to whether America's next crop can
be marketed to the best advantage. Although
money continues abundant and cheap It Is In
evitable that the approaching Government bond
Issue should absorb money and harden rates
temporarily at least. There was realizing In
bonds to-day in speculative Issues and the tone
â was heavy. Total sains, $2,190,000.
â  United States old 4's registered and the new
4's were Va per cent lower and the s's Vi per
cent lower in the bid price.

shares, including: Atchlson, 3SO0; do preferred

NEW YORK. June 7.â The Evening Post's
London financial cablegram says: Consols and
similar stocks were pood here to-day on cheap
money. Speculative (tocka wen- easier in view
of the settlement to br,-ln to-morrow. Foreign
stocKs were weak, notably Chileans on the re
ported lfÂ»sue of Â£400.nrx> in Government treasury
bills.
Americans were bought here, but not from
influential quarters. Milwaukee and St. Paul
and high priced stocks were top heavy. Prices

favored by cheap money.
Silver waf flat on a cessation of the Spanish
orders. The Increased importE and the de
creased exports for May are mainly due to the
receipt of more foodstuffs from the United
States and the lessened purchases of goods by
that country.

CHICAGO GRAJN MARKET.
CHICAGO June 7.â The Liverpool market,
which has been steadily declining for several
days, was especially demoralized to-day, and
reported a drop e^-al to 814 c per bushel In
July and 4Hc In September, with the Conti
nental markets also lower. The crop outlook
contained all that could be dealred, with the
grain In large areas ready for the reaper. The
Kansas report for June shows the condition
103â the highest ever reported at this season of
the year. The report set at rest the rumors
about chinch bugs and rust. As a result longs
quickly began to liquidate. There was urgent
short selling, sales on stop loss orders and on
exhausted margins. Under the pressure the
market went off rapidly. The rally, which fol
lowed, was due to the fact that many shorts
were disposed to take advantage of the break
to secure profits. This class of buying was
sufficient in volume to cause a temporary re
action. The Atlantic exports were astonish
ingly heavy, amounting in wheat and flour to
about 1.700,000 bushels. The figures gave the
discouraged biiUs heart enough to take on some
Wheat and caused many shorts to get out and
be In position to contemplate the situation
philosophically for a while. The total stocks In
the world's visible, as compiled by the Chicago
Daily Trade Bulletin and Liverpool Corn Trade
News, is 109.0u0.000 bushels, against 107,000,000
a> year ago. While the crowd were battling
over the small domestic receipts on the one
hand and the bearish figures connected with
the world's stockÃ on the other, a heavy thun
der storm broke on the Board of Trade build
ing that settled the fight. The shorts rushed to
get their Wheat in out of the wet and the
market became quite strong. July closed 2c
and September %c lower.
Selling orders predominated In Oate at the
opening. Later, however, buyers took hold and
the market steadily advam ed until a gain of
He over yesterday's close was made. A slight
reaction took place at the close. July closed
He lower.
After a weak opening Corn became strong
on unfavorable crop news from Kansas. There
was no export, however. This, together with
the decline In Wheat, caused a decline In prices
near the close. July closed He lower.
Heavy grain markets and lower prices for
live hogs weakened Provisions. One or two
heavy Helling orders were on the market, but
on the decline there were plenty of buyers.
July pork closed 15c lower, lard 5c and ribs
2Hc lower.
The leading futures ranged as follows:
Articles â Open. High. Low. Close.
Wheat, No. 4â

HAY AND FEEDSTUFFS.
Buyers of Hay are holding oft, as they gen
erally do when the new crop comes forward,
hoping thereby to supply themselves at lower
prices. But the conditions are different this
year. The crop Is light and the general disposi
tion among growers Is to hold until the fall.
The fancy grades are weak, but the medium
grades are firm, as there Is a keen demand for
them and they are very scarce.
Bran has advanced. Middlings are un
changed.

SAN FRANCISCO MEAT MARKET.
There is a good demand for Hogs, and as.
they are in light supply the feeling is firm and
a further advance is noted. Still higher prices
are expected to prevail shortly. Beef is also
firm. The other descriptions show no change.

THE STOCK MARKET.
A slight improvement in Caledonia carried
the stock up to 30 cents yesterday, but it sub
sequently fell back. ; Otherwise there was no
change In mining stocks. ; . '
In local securities â  Oceanic Steamship ad
vanced to $62 and Hutchlnson Plantation to
$50, and Hawaiian â¢ Commercial declined' to
$22 124.

In the Chollar mine they have placed two
sots of timbers in the main incline above the
1200 station since last report. On the 100-foot
level the upraise above the north drift from
the east crosscut has been stopped, and they
are now engaged in repairing the main drift.
On the tunnel level the upraise from the west
crosscut, 60 feet north of the south line, has
been advanced 7 feft, and is up 2S feet, show
ing bunches of pay ore as they advance, and
from which they have saved 10' tons and 1000
pounds of ore, the car samples of which
showed an average of $8 12 in gold and 16.71
ounces of silver per ton. On the Brunswick
lode the 800-foot level station of shaft No. 1
has been completed, and a Joint south drift
front the east end of the station has been
started, and is now out 10 feet.
In the Potosi mine they have placed two sets
of timbers In the main Incline since last re
port. The Joint Potosl-Bulllon west crosscut
on the tunnel level has been advanced 25 feet
during the week, and Is now out 106 feet. The
face Is in quartz giving low assays. Since the '
Ist instant it has been run solely by the Potosi
Company. On the Brunswick lode, in incline
shaft No. 1, the 800-foot level station and
chutes have been completed and a Joint drift
started south from the east end of the sta
tion. It is out 10 feet in porphyry. 600-foot
l eve l_The main south drift has been extended
27 feet, and is now out 419 feet south of the
PotoFl north line. The face is In low grade
quartz.

THE LITTLE ENCYCLOPEDIA.
The eye of a dragon-fly contains 28,00(
polished lenses.
Flies are not the only thing found ir
amber. In a big mass of clear amber
dredged up out of the Baltic Sea re
cently, there was distinctly visible in
Its interior a small squirrel â fur, teeth
and claws intact.
It is said that the phonograph is now
used in the Spanish telephone service.
Messages are spoken into it, and the
instrument repeats them to the tele
phone. They are also received on a
phonograph at the distant station.
The microphone is being utilized for
the education of deaf-mutes, and such
persons can actually hear sounds pro
ceeding from the micrnphonograph, and
soon learn to utter them. It appears to
be an efficacious method, even with per
sons who have passed middle life.
M. Leon Lemal has brought out a new
method of coloring glass by covering it
with a silver salt and heating it to 500
degrees centigrade. On cooling it the
glass is found to be tinged. When seen
by light passing through it the glass is
yellow, and it is blue when seen by re
flected light. Salts of gold, iron and
copper and other metals also stain glass
in this way. It is possible to produce
pretty decorations by the process.
One of the greatest wonders of Java,
"the fire island," a large lake of boil
ing mud, is situated almost in the
plains of Grobogana, some distance to
the northeast of Solo. It is nearly two
miles in circumference, and in the cen
ter immense columns of soft, hot mud
may be seen continually, rising and fall-
Ing, like great black timbers thrust
forth an^ then suddenly withdrawn by
a giant's hand. Besides the phenom
enon of the columns, there are two gi
gantic bubbles near the Western edge,
which fill up like huge balloons and ex
plode on an average three times per
minute.

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographlo
Office, located in the Merchants' exchange, la
maintained in San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners without regard to rationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially Invited to visit the
office, where complete rets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
Information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of Interest to ocean commerce.
The timeball on top of the building on Tele
graph Hill Is hoisted about ten minutes befora
noon, and is dropped at noon. 120 th meridian,
by telegraphic signal received each day from
the United States Naval Observatory at Mara
Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the timeball was
dropped on time or giving the error. If any, laj
published the same day by the afternoon papers
and by the mornlnp papers the fo. lowing day.
? P, WELCH. Ensign. U. 3. N.. In charge.

CONSTABLES
Grand Jurors Start on a
New Trail.
RIVAL JUSTICES OF THE PEACE
3NE INVADES THE DOMAIN OF
THE OTHER.
Testimony to Show That an Excess of
Mileage Was Charged on
at Least Two
Occasions.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, June 7.
The Grand Jury has been at work all
day on the affairs of Justices and con
stables. Some of the evidence given before
it evidently needs explanation. Expert
Denehee was at work for a long while on
the records of Justice Clift's court, and it
has been expected all along that some
thin* sensational would result. Since At
torney Kinard commenced his opposition
to the payment of certain fees before tha
Supervisors many facts have been brought
forward to show that a petty and very
undignified fight Is being waged between
the Justices and constables of Oakland
and Brooklyn townships.
Attorney Kinard has two men, E. A.
Carr and J. Ramsey, whose testimony was
given to the Grand Jury to-day. These
men declare that they were arrested at
Sixteenth street depot and that the con
stables swore to charges for mileage from
Highlands, which is many miles further
away. It is also alleged that these two
men were arrested inside the city limits,
and that consequently Justice Clift ille
gally charged fees for trying their cases.
The records in Justice Clift's office show
that Carr and Ramsey were arrested May
31 for vagrancy. The warrant shows that
they were arrested out6ide of the corpo
rate limits of the city of Oakland. They
pleaded guilty and were sentenced to
three days apiece in the County Jail, and
Judge Clift says that everything is per
fectly clear and legal.
Attorney Kinard went to the County
Jail Saturday with another gentleman
who administered an oath to them, the
nature of which no one seems to know
and s'.nce their release Mr. Kinard says
he is keeping them so that they can give
all the testimony necessary to the Grand
Jury. On the other hand, the constables
of Justice Clift's court declare they have
a grievance because of the amount of
criminal business taken to the court in
Brooklyn Township. The cases of Stacey
Rogers of Berkeley, charged with murder
of a young man named Fraser of Berke
ley, charged with felony, and another
criminal case, have been ordered by the
District Attorney to the Brooklyn court
and there tried, thus greatly increasing
the mileage of witnesses and putting them
to great inconvenience. Justice Lawrence
It is stated, has an office close to the
< ounty building and entirely outside of
his township, where he performs as many
marriages and does as much business as
can be steered his way. One of the con
stables of Brooklyn Township, C. S. Wel
don has his office on Washington street
in the heart of Oakland, and it is claimed
that much of the business that should be
done by the constables of Oakland Town
ship is done by the Brooklyn constable
Many other petty charges are being
nung from one camp to the other, and it
is very evident that justice Is for the time
being completely subordinated to politics
t J le amenity that is supposed to
Y^tA, het *'*â¢ Professional men and office
holders has long since vanished, if indeed

BERKELEY WOMEN
WERE OVERLOOKED
SAY THEY FIRST SUGGESTED
THE HOSPITAL SHIP.
Members of the Red Cross Say
That They Have Been
Slighted.
BERKELEY, June 7.-When the ladies i
of the Berkeley branch of the Red Cross
met this morning there was but one topic \
of discussion. Without wishing it to be
made public, they have prided themselves
M far upon being the first to suggest the â 
Idea of the hospital ship, and when this
mornings papers spoke of the subject in
a way which implied that the honor be- i
longed to Oakland they felt that their !
interest and activity in the undertaking ;
had been overlooked.
At an informal conference of the more â 
prominent members cailed to consider the !
question it was decided that the facts of i
the case should be given to the press in
order that the position of the Berkeley i
Society might be understood.
Mrs. Mary S. Haight, one of the pro- !
moters of the Red Cross movement in i
Berkeley, said to a Call representative j
after the meeting was over:
"It occasioned us no little surprise, I as- I
sure you, to read in this morning's paper
that the name of the Oakland branch of
the Red Cross Society should be credited
with so much In connection with this idea
of the hospital ship. Ihe Berkeley branch
was the first local organization of the
kind started upon the coast, and it was j
the first to promote the plan of a hospital
Bhlp. We have never tried to make this |
public before, but It seems only right and i
just that our place should be recognized. \
"The idea of the hospital ship originated
â¢with Mrs. Colonel C. R. Greenleaf, presi- ,
dent of our local society. She spoke of
the matter to us over a month ago. The
flret public announcement of any such
plan was made by Professor Thomas R.
Bacon of the university, who spoke of it |
at a meeting of the State Red Cross So
ciety in San Francisco. He did so as a
representative of our local branch. At
the first meeting of the State board our
delegate from Berkeley was instructed to
pledge $500 as the amount we would con
tribute, and that was over two weeks
ago. We have already succeeded in rais
ing nearly $700. So that both In being the
first to suggest the plan and the largest
contributor of any one local branch so
far we certainly stand In a position
which ought not properly to be over- |
looked, and we hope henceforth that that :
position will be accorded to us."
Mrs. C. R. Greenleaf, whose husband.
Colonel Greenleaf, is now with the United
States troops at Tampa, also expressed
surprise at the prominence accorded the
Oakland branch In the matter which she
was the first to suggest.
"The hospital ship," she said, "was
something which originated with us. We
have not tried to make this public at all,
but the honor is ours. For Its size and
numbers our society has really done very
well, but we have tried to keep it as quiet
as possible."
Several other ladles were unanimous In
regretting that their own society seemed
to have been overlooked In an honor
which belonged peculiarly to them, but
hoped that the mistake could be cor
rected.
DIPLOMAS FOR BLIND PUPILS.
Interesting Exercises at the Institu
tion for the Deaf and Dumb
and Blind.
BERKELEY, June 7.â Twelve pupils of
the State Institution for the Deaf and Dumb
and Blind have finished their course of
study and to-day received the diplomas
which the Institution awards to Us grad
uates. The commencement exercises were
held this afternoon in the Assembly Hall
and consisted principally of an exhibition
of what the pupils themselves had
learned, together with an explanation of
tfce method used In teaching them. Prin
cipal Wilkinson outlined very carefully
to the visitors the way in which the
:ourse of instruction was carried on, and
illustrated It by several examples of class
ALAMEDA COUNTY NEWS.
room work. The address to the graduates,

of Oakland, was Interpreted by Principal

in sign language.

noon.

when she was ejected with violence.

THE CITY TRUSTEES.
Railroad Crossing Franchise Wanted
on Webster Street â Garbage
Crematory Matter.
ALAMEDA, June 7.â At the meeting of
the Board of City Trustees last night an
application was presented for a franchise
from the South Pacific Coast Railroad to
put a crossing over Webster street, near
the estuary bridge. An ordinance to that
effect was Introduced and was referred to
the City Attorney to be acted upon at the
next meeting.
The Pacific Improvement Company sent
a communication to the board offering to
donate a strip of land of sufficient width
to widen Webster street to eighty feet,
on condition that when the street shall be
improved that company shull only be re
quired to pay its just and legal share of
the cost of such Improvement.
Attorney Dodge of Oakland addressed
the board upon the subject of the pro
posed garbage crematory, taking strong
ground against it. After he had spoken
some time he was informed that the mat
ter was not before the board at present
for consideration, whereupon he thanked
the Trustees for their attention and with
drew.
A protest was made against the Pound
master staking his horses out to graze
upon the unimproved public streets, while
threatening to impound the horses of any
other person who dared to do tne same
thing. The Chief of Police was notified to
see that the law was enforced.

Brigham Young's Funeral.
ALAMEDA, June 7.â The funeral of the
late Brigham H. Young took place from
his late residence on Schiller street this
afternoon. There was a large assem
blage of friends of the deceased. The
services were conducted by E. H. Nye of
the California branch or the Mormon
church. A number of elders of the church
were also present and assisted in the
ceremonies, which were after the Mormon
ritual. Interment was la Mountain View
Cemetery.

THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, JUNE 8, 1898.
MYSTERY OF
A THRASHING
John Flemming Held Up
by Masked Men.
SAYS IT WAS EXPECTED.
WITNESSES TO THE AFFAIR DO
NOT UNDERSTAND.
The Victim Was Not Anxious to
Have the Alleged Robbers Aj
rested and Was Not
Robbed.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call
90S Broadway, June 7.
John Flemming, who resides at 40'
Ninth street, had an experience this
morning that contains many elements ol
mystery. About ten minutes after mid
night he was talking at the door of the
Seaman residence to Mrs. Seaman and hex
daughter. As he was bidding them good
night or good-morning he was admon
ished by the young ladies to be careful
not to be held up by footpads. HÂ«
laughingly replied that he did not care, but
had not gone a block before he was con
fronted by two fellows with masks.
Flemming struggled with the smaller
one and yelled lustily, while both men
showered blows upon his head with theii
fists. The Seamans heard his shouting,
and putting their heads out of their win
dows yelled also. Two men who were ap
proaching the corner of Ninth and Web
ster streets heard the shouts and saw the
scuffling, and at once ran to the relief of
Flemming. As soon as they approached
the masked men ran in one direction and
Flemming, who had held on to the hat ol
one of them, ran another way.
The strangers, who undoubtedly by
their coming on the scene saved Flem
ming from more brutal treatment, were
Martin D. Bullion and Horace Black.
They stopped Flemming and told him
that if hÂ« would run for an officer they
would keep the footpads in sight. To
their great surprise Flemming told them
he was not anxious to have the men ar
rested, but preferred to go home without
any fuss. As Flemming was wearing
some jewelry, including diamonds, which
could have been snatched from him in
less time than it took to thrash him. it
was apparent that robbery was not the
motive, although Flemmlng affects to be
lieve that it was. He told Black and Bul
lion that he had been expecting these so
called footpads for several nights, and
conveyed the impression that it was not
altogether a surprise.
To a detective who visited his home this
morning the young man said that he was
held up by two robbers, one of whom
thrashed him, while the other attempted
to stuff something down his throat. Flem
ming said that when the strangers ap
proached they did not hurry, because they
thought, and so told him, that it was
merely a friendly scuffle. Bullion denies
this, and says they ran as fast as possi
ble to Flemming's relief, and were very
much surprised that he did not wish
them to follow up the alleged robbers.
The police investigated the story to-day
and nave come to the conclusion that
there is some other motive than robbery
for the assault made upon Mr. Flemming.
T. C. Williams Will Not Remain.
OAKLAND. June 7.â The Rev. Theodore
C. Williams, who came here six months
ago in the place of Rev. C. W. Wendte,
pastor of the Unitarian church, has noti
fied that organization that he will refuse
to accept a longer term. He only con
sented to stay six months when he came
here from New York city. His health was
poor and he .doubted the effect of this cli
mate and his fears have proven correct,
for he is not able to keep up his work.
The members of the church were well
pleased with his pastorate and had hoped
to retain him. The pulpit committee is
now considering his successor.
Showed Phoenix No Leniency.
OAKLAND, June 7.â Willis Phoenix,
the mulatto convicted Saturday of burg
lary in tapping a Southern Pacific till at
the foot of Broadway, was sentenced to
five years at Folsom Prison by Judge Og
den to-day. To the jury's recommenda
tion for leniency the Judge remarked:
"Why the jury made such a recommenda
tion I can't see. There was nothing in
the testimony to Indicate that the de
fendant was guiltless. He had been con
victed of previous burglaries, showing
that he is a dangerous man."
Tallied to a Cent.
OAKLAND, June 7.â At the request of
County Clerk Jordan the funds in his
possession were counted to-day by Chair
man Talcott and Supervisors Church and
Mitchell. The various accounts showed
that the Clerk should have $4703 46 in the
treasury, and the money in the safe ex
actly balanced the amount. On June 7 of
last year the amount in the County
Clerk's safe was $4359 26. This money is
partly taken in as fees, and partly held In
the interest of estates in probate.
Two Husbands Granted Divorces.
OAKLAND, June 7.â Judge Ogden to
day severed the conjugal relations exist
ing between Joseph N. Railton and Fan
nie M. Railton on the ground of w;lllful
desertion. The husband was the plaintiff.
The application of Hans Lanbeck for a

granted. The plaintiff was given the cus

of the growing crop on 300 acres in Wash
ington Township.
Combination of Milk Men.
OAKLAND, June 7. â The Central
Creamery has been incorporated to carry
on a general milk business. The Talcott,
the Carr, the California and the Medau
dairies have consolidated in this corpora
tion. The idea is to reduce expenses in
men, wagons and teams. Several of the
larger dairies have not gone and will not
go into the combination.

Moving Passion Play Pictures.
Commencing to-morrow afternoon, and]
continuing every evening and afternoon
thereafter for a limited period, will be
given a revival of the great "Passion
Play" pictures, which created Quite a stir
when they were shown at the Baldwin
Theater a few weeks since. The manage
ment of the Baldwin has had so man>
requests of late asking for a revival of
the play that arrangements have been
completed, and the play dealing with the
birth, life and death of Christ will once
more be shown in all its phases, exactly
as It is given every ten year* in the
mountains of Horitz by the peasants of
Oherammergau. Kach scone is shown
with vividness and perfect realism, and
the accompanying music and lectures add
materially to the effect of the whole.
When the play was last given evening
performances were omitted, and as there
were many who were not able to attend
the afternoon performances the coming
presentations of the play at night will no
doubt be well attended. The play will be
given every afternoon and evening, in
cluding Sunday. Reserved seats for mati
nee or night ;ire 25 cents for adults and
15 cents for children.

At the annual election of officers of the

organization, delivered an address on
civil service reform.
