the organization to contribute the sum
of $10 per month for the benefit of the
Red Cross Society..
Adam Stroh Pleads Guilty.
Adam Stroh, the burglar who, on last
Christmas night, entered the residence of
Charles S. Bier, Geary and Franklin
streets, pleaded guilty to a charge of bur
glary in the first degree before Judge
Borden yesterday. The prisoner waived
time, but, in order that evidence might be
produced to show that the prisoner had a
prior conviction against him, sentence
was postponed until June 18. Stroh's
crime created a sensation at the time ft
was committed, owing to the fact that
Baldwin Gardner was fatally shot while
the burglar was trying to escape.

FOUR CENTS
FOR A MEAL
Chief Fletcher Does Not
Spoil Prisoners.
A RECORD FOR ECONOMY
BEATS ALL TESTS BECENTLY
MADE BY GOVERNMENT.
Does Not Serve French Dinners, but
Has a Healthy Lot of In
mates in the
Prison.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
_Â,,, Broadway, June 7.
Chief of Police Fletcher claims to have
broken the record In the economical feed
ing of prisoners. His report filed last
night shows that during the past month
he has supplied two square meal^ a day
to his prisoners at an average price of
4 cents a meal.
This low flgura is said to bÂ« smaller
than the average price for feeding pris
oners in any other jail in the United
State*. For feeding all his prisoners dur
ing the month of May the Chief only ex
pended $99 80.
Everybody employed at the City Hall
has an Interest In the feeding of the pris
oners. Cnly a few weeks ago a special
resolution was passed by the Board of
Public "Works, authorizing Chief Fletcher
not to permit cabbages or onions to be
cooked in the prison in the basement on
Tuesdays or Wednesdays, because on
those days the board holds it 3 regular
session. The board did not show any
consideration for the feelings of the City
Council, which meets every Monday night,
and hence the bill of fare on Monday
always includes cabbages and onions.
"Some time ago," said the Chief to-day,
"I was considerably interested in some
Government experiments tending to
prove that an average person could live
very well on 18 cents a day. My experi
ence in the past month has taught me
that I could serve French dinners at that
price. I hope the people will not think
that we starve prisoners here because we
try not to waste money. Here is the
amount of material consumed In one
week: May 1, mutton, 45 pounds; bread,
35 pounds. May 2, fresh beef, 45 pounds;
bread, 35 pounds. May 3, corned beef, 40
pounds; bread, 30 pounds. May 4, fresh
beef, 40 pounds; bread, 30 pounds. May
5, beef, 40 pounds, bread. 35 pounds. May
6, beef, 45 pounds; bread, 35 pounds. May
7, corned beef, 40 pounds; bread, 30
pounds. /
"In addition to these amounts, wo fur
nished the prisoners with bean soup, cof
fee, once a day, use a large amount of
cabbage and onions and give the boys
rice or bean soup every day except Mon
days and Fridays. As bean soup sends a
nice flavor in the air we cook it on the
days when the Police Commission meets.
"We serve our guests two meals a day,
at 8 a. in. and 4 p. m., and so far as I
can learn they are as well satisfied as in
any prison in the State."
STOLE TWO FUR CAPES.
Fred Vendal, Alias Blum, Alleged
Leader of a Gang of Shop
lifters, Arrested.
Fred Vendal, alias Blum, who, the po
lice say, is the leader of a gang of shop
lifters, was booked at the City Prison
yesterday on a charge of petty larceny,
second offense, which makes It a felony.
He was identliled In the prison yester
day by a clerk In the fur store of Lach
man & Co., 110 Kearny street. A few days
ago, accompanied by a woman, Vendal
visited the store and pretended that he
wanted to purchase a fur cape. He and
the woman looked at several, but none
suited them and they left. After they
had gone it was noticed that two of the
capes had disappeared. The woman is
known as Joe Theron's girl. Theron is
now waiting his trial on a charge of rob-
I bery. The clerk failed to identify the
woman.
Yendal is a Russian and has served
terms for petty larceny. The police say
that he and the woman have worked
other stores in the same way, and other
; charges may yet be preferred against
: them. Vendal was arrested by Detective
! Graham and Policeman J. Moriarlfy.
A New Hebrew School.
The Hebrew Religion School (Talmud
Torah) of the Nevah Zedek Congregation,
on Mission street, haa been opened, with
Rabbi N. Mosessohn, D.D., LL.D., as
principal. Instruction Is given In the He
brew language, history and religion. The
latter two subjects are taught in English.
The tuition hours are: On Sundays, from
9a. m. to 12 m. ; Mondays, Tuesdays,
Wednesdays and Thursdays, from 3:30 p.
m. to 5:30 p. m. Children are accepted
by the principal. Children of parents who
are unable to pay for tuition are admit
ted free. Rabbi Mosessohn addresses the
pupils every Sunday on religious topics.
Rev. Joseph Riordan Will Lecture.
The next course of free- lectures given by
the Young Men's Institute Lecture Bureau
will be delivered under the auspices of
Borromean Council No. 129 at St. Ignatius
College Hall, Friday evening, June 24.
Rev. Joseph W. Riordan, S. J., president
of Santa Clara College, will be the lec
turer, taking for his subject "Evolution."
Tickets of admission may bo had free
upon application at the office of the lec
ture bureau, second floor. Pioneer build-
Ing, 24 Fourth street.

Mrs. Johnson's Statement.
The widow of Otto Johnson, who shot
and killed himself last Monday night,
6ays that she was not aware that he had
a pistol on his person when he left the
house lust before committing the deed.
She din not know that he had left the
house until after he had been gone some
time.

RED CROSS'
UNTIRING
WORKERS
Some Who Labor for
the Cause.
LADIES MAKE SACRIFICES
THE FUND NOW NEARING THE
$50,000 MARK.
Preparing to Give lowa's Represen
tatives a Grand Reception.
Other Interesting
Notes.
Mrs. I. Lowenberg, chairman of
the Hospitality Committee of the
local Red Cross Society, announces
that the committee -will now be lo
cated at the Tiburon ferry depot,
and that owing to the limited quar
ters for the troops admission will
be secured only by ticket.
Mrs. Lowenberg also appeals for,
lunches for the 500 to iKX) soldiers to
arrive to-morrow and the 800 ex
pected on Friday.
Mention has been frequently made in
The Call of the grand work that has been
In progress during the past few months
under the auspices of the Red Cross So
ciety and also of the sacrifices that have
been made in behalf of the boys in blue
by the ladies who compose the well
known organization; but The Call now
takes pleasure in presenting the names of
some of the active members of the soci
ety, who have devoted their time exclu
sively to the noble work.
There are many thousands of both sexes
In this city and State who have done their
share in assisting the society to carry out
its mission of mercy, and due credit has
been given of their efforts in The Call.
in presenting the list, many names are
lacking, but it is due to the fact that hun
dreds have in a quiet and unostentatious
way aided the various committees in car
rying out their work, and their names are
unknown.
Following is a list of officers and com
mittees of the local society:

The game to be played between the Thir
teenth Regiment. Minnesota Volunteers,
and the San Francisco Club of the Pacific
Cnast Baseball League to-morrow for the
benefit of the Red Cross Society promises
to be a very exalting and "interesting
event. The players that will compose the
regimental team have played with some
of the best professional leagues in the
country.
The ladies of the Red Cross Society
promise to make this a society event, and
the entertainment committee have post
poned all other entertainments that were
to have taken place this week. Mayor
Phelan will probably toss the first ball to
the pitcher on the occasion in honor of
the visitors.
The Hamilton Grammar School has
been particularly generous to the soldier!
and to the Red Cross. : Besides sending
750 lunches to Fontana Barracks ' thi
school donated $S2 to the fund and alB(
pledged itself to pay $60 a month durinf
the present war. Comfort bags and othe:
useful necessities have been donated b:
them since the work of aiding the sol"
diers began. . !
lowa's pride, the. Fifty-first Regiment
Is expected to arrive to-morrow, and th<
ladies who have gladdened the hearts o

CAMP MBRRITT, SAN FRANCISCO, Cal.,
June 4. I*9S.
President Ladies' Red Cross Society. San
Francisco, Cal. â Dear Madam: We were much
surprised and gratified last evening to receive
from you a package containing- caps for the
officers and also a case of fresh eggs. The
latter have been awarded by lot to two of the
companies, furnishing an ample supply for
breakfast for both. The other articles we shall
take with us, and have no doubt that . the
future will give us ample occasion for appre
ciating your kind forethought. Sincerely yours,
C. W. REEVE, Colonel Commanding.

WILL ASSIST THE
RED CROSS WORK
MECHANICS' INSTITUTE TO
SHOW PATBIOTISM.
Prospects of a Good Entertainment at
the Pavilion for the Benefit of
Soldiers Soon to Embark.
The present war with Spain has given
aoth citizens? and public Institutions every
opportunity to show their patriotism, and
In none Is this more manifest than the
Mechanics' Institute. When the first
volunteers left this coast they were amply
supplied with good literature by this in
stitution, and from time to time the
board of directors has, in behalf of the
membership, manifested Its patriotism In
various ways.
At the regular meeting of the board of
directors last night the time was largely
ijiven up to the discussion of methods of
rendering life mure pleasant to the vol
unteers. A letter waa received fr-jm a
French society stating that it Intended
giving the proceeds of the regular 14th of
July celebration to the Red Cross Society,
and the use of the Pavilion at one-third
the regular rates was at once granted the
society. Mr. Wilson then suggested, that
the Institute itself might do something in
the entertainment line for the Red Cross
Society, and a committee consisting of
Messrs. Fraser, Wilson, McLaren and
Healy was appointed to make preliminary
irrangements. It i 6 probable that a mili
tary demonstration will be held some
time during the month, the gross receipts
Df which will be devoted to the Red Cross
On the suggestion of Mr. Jelllson, 11
brarlnn of the Institute, the free use of
the rooms was extended to all soldiers In
uniform, and a quantity of books will
be sent with the next troopsh'ps.
The quarterly reports of the secretary
and librarian were of a very satisfactory
nature. While there has not been a great
In membership there has been no ap
preciable loss, and the financial standing
Is better than ever. There are at present
!2!<2 persons on the membership roll. The
regular quarterly meeting of the institute
will be held on Saturday evening.
NEW JAPANESE CONSUL.
Distinguished Diplomats Arrive in
This City.
The new Japanese Consul, Count F.
Mutsi, arrived on the Belgic yesterday af
ternoon. Though a young man, the new
Consul has seen some service In the diplo
matic corps of his country which entitles
him to a high ranking. He was formerly
attached to the legation at Washington.
Accompanying Count Mutsi is Dr. B.
Tomatsuri, a surgeon in the Japanese
army. He is here on a visit to Investigate
the methods employed by the United
States Government in the mobilization of
Its troops and the preparation for the Ma
nila expedition.

PROPOSALSâ For the repair of bituminous
rock pavententsâ of the Clerk of the
Board Of Supervisors of the City and County
of San Francisco, June. S, ISSS.
In accordance with resolution No. 1242 (fourth
series) of the Board of Supervisors, SEALED
PROPOSALS will be received in open session
of the board on MONDAY AFTERNOON,
June 20. ISSS, from 2:30 to 3 o'clock, for repair-
ing and keeping in good order and condition,
to the entire satisfaction of the Superintendent
of Streets and of the Board of Supervisors, all
streets or portion's. of streets. Including street
crossings and intersections, which are now or
may be hereafter, paved with bituminous rock,,
for a term of two (2) years from July 1, lsÂ»S,
to and including June 30, ISOO.
The repairs are to be made in strict accord-
ance- with â¢ specifications on file and open for
Inspection in this office. Â»
. A list of streets and street crossings, etc.,
now paved with bituminous rock, and which
will require UÂ», be kept In repair, as above |
stated.; is also on file and' open [or inspection i
in this office,' and all streets or portions of I
streets paved with- bituminous rock and accept-
ed by the city and county, are Included and
must be put in good condition and kept in !
thorough - repair, whether they are recited in
said list or not. All streets and street cross-
Ings enumerated in said list must be put in
good repair â  and condition within a period of
three months from award- of contract, and
must be kept in good repair until the expira-
tion of the contract; in addition to all streets
or portions of streets paved with bituminous
rock and accepted by the city and county dur-
ing the continuance of. the contract, except
only such streets or portions of streets where
a bond has been given and Is In force for keep-
ing the same in repair; also, those portions of
streets required by law to be kept in repair by
the street railway companies having tracks
thereon.
' NOTlCEâ Bidders must state a specific sum
for which the entire work will be performed,
with the understanding that monthly payments
will be made therefor to the extent of one-
twenty-fourth of the sum bid, on a certificate
from- the Superintendent of Streets, approved
by the Board of Supervisors, that the work is
being satisfactorily performed.
Bidders will also be required to file with
their bid a description of and the character of
the plant owned by them, with a chemical an-
alysis and a sample of the bituminous rock to
be used in carrying out and performing the
work under this contract.
And it Is to be distinctly understood that
eight hours shall constitute a day's work for
pecsons employed on the above work, and that
the minimum rate of wages to be paid shall
be two ($2) dollars per day; also, that the
amount of, the bond to be given will not be
less than one-half the amount bid for the per-
formance of this work.
No bid will be received or entertained from
any person, firm or corporation who does not
own a fully, equipped plant suiflcient for the
performance" of this work.
The party to whom the contract Is awarded
will be required prior to or at. the time of tfie
execution of said contract, to pay the cost of
advertising this notice in three dally news-
papers. â¢ â¢â .:â¢â¢ -
â¢. la order to preserve uniformity, and to facil-
itate the award, the board has resolved to re-
ceive- no bids unless made upon blank forms
prepared by the committee, and a ' certified
check for the sum of five thousand (s3o<io) dol-
lars In favor of and made payable to the Clerk
of the board, must be deposited by the bid-
der, conditioned. If the proposal Is accepted and
the contract awarded, and if the bidder shall
fail or neglect to execute a written agreement
and give the bond required within six (6) days
after the award is made, then; and In that
case, the said sum shall be paid Into the city
and county treasury by said Clerk as liqui-
dated damages for such failure and neglect.
Blanks furnished by the Clerk.
The board reserves the right to reject all
bids It the public good so require.

AT STAKE
The Valentine Fortune
in Litigation.
A NEW YORK BROTHER SUES
LIGHT ON THE AFFAIRS OF THE
BICH PRINTER.
A Legal Battle Over the Rancho
Corte Madera del Presidio
in the County of
' Marin.
One of the most Important land Bults
filed in Marin County for years Is that
of M. B.Valentine of New York againsi
Jennie A. Valentine and S. D. Valen
tine. It is a claim to an undivided half
of 1300 acres known as the Rancho
Corte Madera del Presidio.
Defendants axe executrix and execu
tor respectively of the will of the late
Thomas B. Valentine, founder of the
well known San Francisco firm of
Francis, Valentine & Co. Plaintiff is a
prominent lawyer and ex-Judge In New
York. His attorneys are Messrs, Grant.
Mullany and Cushing of this city!
Speaking of the case yesterday Mr.
Cushing said:
"The suit calls for an undivided half
of an undivided fourth of the well
known Rancho Cort^ Madera del Pre
sidio, Marin County, which consists of
more than 1300 acres; also of such por
tions of the original tract as may have
been conveyed by the late Thomas Val
entine to his wife in his lifetime to
gether with plaintiffs share in the is
sues, rents and profits of the lands in
question.
"The land was bought by Thomas B
\ alentine in 1868 from one Bolton Our
contention is that half the purchase
money was advanced by M. B. Valen
tine, the title being put in the name of
Thomas B. Valentine for good and law
ful reasons. We hold that the late
Thomas B. Valentine held half of the
lands in trust for his brother, and this
fact may be shown hereafter by proper
documentary evidence, though the caÂ«e
may not develop along that line "
PROTESTS WITHDRAWN.
Local Merchants Willing the War
. ship Should Go to Manila.
The remark has been made by some of
the people at Washington that the Pa
cific Coast merchants and manufactur
ers are hard to please, and that they
want everything in sight in the way of
commercial advantages. These remarks
were caused by the fact that on many
occasions the several bodies of the West
have demanded their rights when they
thought that jobbers of the East were
getting more than their share of the good
things that were going, and the Eastern
communities getting better and more fa
vors from the Government than those of
tho West. That the Western people do
not want the earth is manifest by a little
correspondence that has recently passed
between this city and Washington. The
annexed dispatch and answer were re
ceived and sent yesterday by the Cham
ber of Commerce of this city:
Washington, June 6, 1593.
Hon. Hugh Craig, President Chamber
of Commerce: I think the people of Cal
ifornia should protest against sending
from the Pacific Coast the Monterey
Monadnock and all other armored war
vessels. GEORGE C. PERKINS.
San Francisco, June 7. IS9S.
Hon. George C. Perkins, United States
Senate, Washington, U. C: We protested
on the 12th of April. In the meantime
Dewey has acquired for California a new
outlet for our energies, employment for
our shipping, business for our merchants
and opened a colonizing future for Amer
icans beyond our most ardent expecta
tions, and we of San Francisco will hold
nothing back which may be of service
to the administration, even to our jeop
ardy. HUGH CRAIG.
President Chamber of Commerce.
E. Scott, secretary of the chamber, said
that when the Spanish fleet was at large
In the Pacific the members believed that
the commerce in the western ocean was in
danger, and it was because of fear the
Spanish admiral might take a notion to
run over to this coast and pay the cities an
unwelcome visit, that the first protest was
made against lighting ships iHMiurrrmoved
from those waters. Since Dewey cleared
the Pacific in the manner he did of the
Spanish there Is less cause for fear than
before. Still, while the war is In pro
gress, danger is not all passed. Said he:
"When the War Department decided to
Bend the Monterey and the other war
ships to the Philippines the officials no
doubt knew what they were doing, and
they were satisfied that this coast was
comparatively safe. It would ill become
us. who are to receive so many benefits
from the recent movements on the part
of the Government, to make further pro
test against what the Government be
lieves to be a necessity." From the po
sition taken by the members of the Cham
ber of Commerce It is apparent that the
local business men believe that the hold
ing of the Philippine Island*) will be of
great benefit to the people of tn.s State.
In fact, such has practically been ad
mitted. To secure the trade that Is sure
to flow Into this port from the Philip
pines they are willing to get along with
out the protection from Uncle Sam s war
ships than they would demand under dif
ferent circumstances.
HIS LEG BRUISED.
Earl J. King Injured in an Elevator
Accident.
Earl J. King, an elevator boy employed
at Webber's music store, at 225 Sutter
etreet, bud a narrow escape from being
kilted yesterday afternoon. For some rea
son the boy climbed on top of the cage.
and pulled the rope. Instead of lowering
the elevator, as be intended, he found
himself going upward. K.vUlzlng he bad
pulled the wrong rope he b-came "rat
tled" and started to jump through tbe ele
vator door. His left leg was caught In the
floor and severely bruised. The screams
of the injured boy attracted tho attention
of the clerks in the music store and they
went to hie assistance. He was extricated
from his perilous position after consider
able trouble and removed to the office. A
doctor was hastily summoned and he
dressed the Injured leg. When asked by
his father why he had climbed on top of
the elevator cage the boy answered that
he had seen his predecessor "do the name
trick," and that, be was anxious to try It.

ABBOTT'S
ACCOUNTS
CORRECT
Santa Rosalia Mining
Company's Mistake.
SAID COIN WAS EMBEZZLED
ACCUSED A TRUSTED EMPLOYE
AND BUSINESS ASSOCIATE.
Difference Between Mexican and Uni
ted States Money, However, Was
Responsible for the Mis
understanding.
About three months ago a suit. Innocent
! enough in the wording of the complaint,
| but formidable in the amount it sought to
| recover, was filed in this city by the San
' ta Rosalia Mining Company against Au
gustus Abbott, one of the stockholders
i and Its former superintendent. The suit,
i however. It is now determined, meted out
j an injustice to the defendant, and its in
stigation was solely due to the fact that
j the directors of the plaintiff corporation
were either in Ignorance of or did not
: stop to consider the fact that Mexico is &
silver standard country and that two of
its dollars equal only one of the coinage
I of the United States in the commercial
world. Since the directors of the mining
corporation have been apprised of the
fact they have dismissed the suit against
; Mr. Abbott, the dismissal being prompted
by the fact that they ha 4no suit to pros
ecute, as their books balanced to the cent
with those of their former superintendent,
who was immediately exonerated when
the comparison was made.
Abbott was the trusted employe and
business associate of the officers of the
company for about three years in the
management of one of the richest mining
properties owned in San Franciscoâ and
It was not until several months after his
retirement from the position of superin
tendent that the management determined
that suit for the recovery of a large
amount of money must be instituted. The
directors were of the opinion that they
had dug up a deep plot, that irregularities
existed in Abbott's accounts and that he
had left on a tour abroad for the sole
purpose of remaining out of the reach of
processes issuing out of California courts.
That Abbott was absent there could be no
doubt, and owing to this fact suit for the
recovery of $.10 000 was filed against him
and an attachment was issued against j
13.600 shares in the mining corporation, of
which he was owner, held in escrow by
the Bank of California and on the secre
tary's books. No service on Abbott was
made, but he became cognizant of the
facts of the case while traveling, and he
informed his attorney, Colonel W. P.
Harlow of Nogales, Arizona. Abbott was
nonplussed at the action of the directors,
but he continued on his trip, trusting to
Colonel Harlow to see that the complain
ants were properly apprised of the facts.
Colonel Harlow has completed his labors
in behalf of Abbott, has apprised the di
rectors of the facts of the case, ha* re- '
ceived their word that the former snpo'r- !
Intendent does not owe one cent to the !
corporation and is now on his way home, j
"The facts of the transaction and cir- i
cumstanois that so startled the directors I
of the corporation are these," said Colonel
Harlow last evening. "Mr. Abbott was
placed In charge of the mining property,
and he went to Mexico to see that its j
development was properly conducted, and j
it was his desire to obey the laws of Max
ico instead of his wish to disobey the '
laws of the United States that led to the
suit being tiled against him. The laws of
Mexico make it necessary to keep the !
books of all corporations in Spanish. This i
being the fact, when the income from the
mine was balanced it was placed in the
books in figures representing its value in
coin of the realm in which it was pro- ;
duced. For instance, one of the items on
the Mexican book* that roused the sus
picions nf the directors was one of $44,000,
representing a like number of Mexican
coins of that denomination, while the
books of the directors only credited the
output of the mine with $22,000, half the
amount placed to its credit by its super
tnt.-ndent on his accounts. Many similar
condition! were found to exist." and the
directors, when they received the books
from Mexico, immediately concluded that I
a shortage existed. Mr. Abbott trusted to ,
the knowledge of the directors regarding !
the difference of monry values to set I
tln-m right regarding the accounts, but '
in their anxiety to protect themselves !
against loss they did not stop to consmer
the condition of affairs.
"Tn the meantime. Mr. Abbott had ar
rived from Mexico and. after marrying
one of Oakland's belles, started on' his
wedding tour. He went to Mexico and ,
then started a tour of the United States i
It wss the tour of his honeymoon that !
too?; him away from the cfty and his !
business which led some of the directors ;
to believe that he was traveling solely to '<
escape summons in thÂ» act, on which has '
been filed against him. He learned of the I
suit, however, and instructed me to care I
for his Interests and Investigate the al- !
legations of the directors. This I did !
Two months ago I went to Mexico and !
Investigated the books of the company i
still remaining there, and learned of the !
output of the mine. I soon became ac- !
ciualnted with the condition that had led i
to the misunderstanding, and returned to I
this city. The matter had In the mcant'me
been placed in the hands of James Me- '
Nabb by the directors for settlement and
together we went over the books of the
company. An expert accountant was .-m
ployed. and when he had concluded his
labors it was found that there was not '<
one cent due from my client. To corrob- I
orate this statement. T have in my pos
session a receipt from the company for all
ltÃ demands against Mr. Abbott The
matter, which created considerable sur
prise at the time, has nroven to have been
based on a film of circumstances which
if the directors had investigated would
have disappeared without even the trouble
incurred by the filing of a complaint for
money thnt was not due."

THE SAN FRAIN CISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, JUNE 8, 1898.
ARTISTS WILL AID SOLDIERS.
The presence of our army has kindled such a feeling of patriotism among
artists and art students that they have given their best work for the exhibition
and sale to be held under the auspices of the Press Club Saturday afternoon
and evening, June 11.
The idea of the benefit was at first conceived of by N. Clark, who at once
appointed the following committee of assistants: Mrs. A. W. Scott, Annabel
I,ee and Caro Cook. Together these ladies have labored, and now there is no
end of art treasures in the pink room of the Press Club building, awaiting the
coming artistic festival. Hera all varieties of art are representedâ wood carv
ing, etching, china, painting, illustrating and poker drawing, in fact, every cre
ation that the master of the brush and palette idealizes or that the modeler in
clay interpretsâ has been solicited.
Governor Budd yesterday expressed himself as being heart and soul with
the movement. He said: "Nothing but sickness will prevent my being with you
Saturday, and even in that event I shall be with you in spirit."
So far all of the art schools in the city have been represented, including work
by students from the Hopkins, Lfitlmer, Jorgensen, Solly Walter and Partington
schools. The members of the Press Club will furnish the refreshments, and
Frank Coffin is preparing an excellent musical programme. The pictures that
remain from the afternoon sale will bo auctioned off by S. M. Shortridge in the
evening.
SAY THEY
HAVE BEEN
SOLD OUT
Democrats Complain
of Neglect.
IMPROPER REPRESENTATION
DISTRICT SOUTH OF MARKET
WAS OVERLOOKED.
Probability That the State Central
Committee Will Decide Against
Fusion With the Populists
and Silver Republicans.
The rebellion In the ranks of the local
Democracy, caused by the manner in
which the new City and County Com
mittee ha 6 been foisted upon the party,
gains strength as the days go by.
When the McNab-Gould-Alford com
bination elected to reform the Demo
cratic party of San Francisco and to
appoint a committee of political "high
Joints" of their own selection to take
the affairs of the party in hand in the
interest of Mayor Phelan there went
forth a murmur of disapproval from a
majority of the Democrats in the city,
none c-f whom had been taken into the
confidence of the professional reform
ers. Since the action of the trium
virate has been considered in connec
tion with the Mayor's alleged assertion
that he had decided to turn his back
on that part of the Democracy that
casts its votes in the districts south of
Market street this murmur has grown
into a roar that threatens to engulf
those responsible for the present con
dition of things.
The investigation that has been
made during the past few days shows
that those Assembly districts which re
turned Democratic majorities at the
last general election are given but a
very small percentage erf representa
tion in the new City and County Com
mittee. This percentage is so small,
in fact, that they have an aggregate
of only twenty-seven members in the
committee, while the districts which
returned Republican majorities have
a total representation of seventy-three.
The banner Democratic district in
the city is the Thirty-first. At the last
general election this district railed up
a Democratic majority of 919. The
Democrats living In this district be
lieved themselves entitled to much con
sideration when it came to the selec
tion of the men who are to have the
authority to direct the affairs of the
party. Unfortunately for them the dis
trict is situated on the Bouth side of
Market street, and, being so, was not
considered eligible to more than a
whisper in the councils of the reformed
Democracy. The district was given
two places on the City and County
Committee.
On the other Bide of Market street is
the Fortieth Assembly District, which
at the last general election returned a
Republican majority of 809. From this
district seventeen aristocrats were se
lected to serve on the City and County
Committee. The Twenty-eighth, Thir
ty-second and Thirty-third Assembly
districts were given one committeeman
each, although at the last general elec
tion they returned Democratic majori
ties of 379, 405 and 174, respectively. On
the other side of Market street the
Thirty-seventh was given thirteen
committeemen and the Thirty-ninth
eleven, although they are both Repub
lican districts.
The Democratic State Central Com
mittee, content with its marvelous paci
fication of the party in this city, is
making preparations for a stormy ses
sion next Saturday. The question of
nxing the time and place for the hold
ing of the State convention is to be de
cided, and the decision will hinge upon
the question of whether or not the
party will fuse with the Populists and
Silver Republicans. There seems to be
a general feeling against fusion among
the Democrats throughout the State,
and the same is true of the local Dem
ocracy. The party leaders believe that
fusion tends to weaken the party as a
party, and that thousands of old line
Democrats who always vote the party
ticket straight under any and all cir
cumstances consider tht-msHves at lib
erty to wander from the straight and
narrow path as soon as the ticket
ceases to be a party ticket and becomes
a fusion ticket. They also say that
a desire for fusion is a confession of
weakness.
A glance at the following table will
show why a majority of the Democrats
in the city are kicking against the new
order of things:
Assembly District.

It will bo Been that the only excep
tion to the general rule was in the
Forty-fifth Assembly District. This is
accounted for by the fact that this is
McNab's district and the nine men who
were appointed from the district are
his personal friends. Even this excep
tion is a poor one, for the district is
on the north side of Market street and
the Democratic majority Riven at the
last election was a small one.
There are others who hold a con
trary view and contend that the point
to be attained is the acquisition of
votes, the manner In which they are
secured being but a secondary consid
eration. Such men as Nathan Cole Jr.
and A. Caminetti are ministers of this
doctrine, and are doing all in their
power to brine: about a general ac
ceptance of their views. They are sup
ported by such of the Populists as be
lieve that half a loaf is better than no
bread. They R P sÂ«rt that it is better to
side with the stronger party on the sil
ver question, and incidentally to ac
cept such of the minor offices as may
be doled out to them as a sop to their
loss of dignity, than to be martyrs to
principles that are fast becoming to be
considered questionable
The Populists of the Cator following,
who are indignant because of the at
titude of the Democrats regarding the
primary election law, Â»as well as the
treatment they received at the hands of
their allies after the last general elec
tion, are doing everything within their
power to prevent fusion during the
coming campaign. They hope, by
standing nlone, to secure the balance
of power in the next Legislature and
thus be in a position to force Demo
crats and Republicans nlike to yield up
some of the good things.
From present indications It seems
crrtnin that the Stote Central Com
mittee will deride against fusion and
that the convention' will not b^ hold
until some time Jn August or the early
part of September.
THE REFEREE BEAT HIM.
Jack Ennis Defeated by Billy Otts at
the Jefferson Athletic Club.
The JofOrson Athletic Club gave Its
monthly boxing entertainment last even
inÃ in Mowry's Hall and a fairly good
crowd was present. The preliminaries,
which were rather tame, created but lit
tle interest. They consisted of throe and
ff T Ou r, r ? und b Â°uts between C. Conway and
J. McDonald, Mortimer Meyers and J.
Hayes, A. Hennlng and J. O'Connor and
Joseph McDonald and E. Wiley.
The event of the evening was an right
round contest between Elll'y Otts and Jack
I-iiiiils, which should certainly have been '
a victory for Erfhis, but the referee. John
McCormlck, is either totally ignorant of
the rules of boxing or else ho went into
the ring with the Intention of awarding
the fight to Otts unless Knnis scored a
knock-out, which performance he came
within an ace of accomplishing.
Excepting the first and fourth rounds,
which were ended in favor of Otts. Ennis
bad certainly the better of the six rounds
fought and the very least- he should have
been entitlod to was a draw.
During the contest Referee MeCormiek i
favored Otts by throwing his entire j
weight on Ennis when separating the
contestants, thus proving beyond question I
that he was In favor of Otts winning the
mill. If the Jefferson Club will tolerate
this system of refereeing fights it can be
safely said that the members will soon
grow weary of the game and allow the
club to die a quiet death. ' k
HOW TO RIDE
IN THE PARK
New Rules Adopted by
the Commissioners.
FOR DRIVERS AND BIKERS
A LIBERAL LICENSE GIVEN THE
PUBLIC.
Simple and Practical Directions by
Observing Which People May
Escape Arrest, Fines and
Imprisonment.
At a special meeting of the Park
Commissioners held yesterday after
noon, Commissioners Joseph Austin
and A. B. Spreckels present, the safety
and convenience of the public were
considered by the adoption of the fol
lowing rules, which will be of the
greatest interest to all cyclists, drivers
and the people in general:
Section 1. Bicycles, tricycles, velocipedes
and vehicles of similar character may be
ridden or driven over any and all the
driveways in Golden Gate Park and. on
and over the boulevard or great highway,
except the driveway around Stow Lake
and the driveway leading up to Straw
berry Hill and the carriage concourse ad
jacent to the concert station and the
speed road and the main drive where par
alleled by the bicycle road.
The exclusion from the carriage con
course aforesaid is limited to the after
noons of days when there is music at the
established concert station.
Sec. 2. All persons are prohibited from
riding or driving any bicycle, tricycle or
velocipede on or over any of the lawns or
footpaths in Golden Gate Park, except
said vehicles may be led over the short
walk leading from Page-street entrance
to the south drive, and also over the walk
leading from the corner of Fulton and
Stanyan streets to the North Ridge ropd.
Sec. 3. Not more than two bicycles, tri
cycles or velocipedes going in the same di
rection at the same rate of speed shall be
ridden abreast.
Sec. 4. All vehicles named in this ordi
nance must be driven in a straightaway
course aa nearly as possible, and must
not run or be driven crosswise, curving to
or fro. Wheelmen must conform to the
customary rules of the road. In meeting
a vehicle going in the opposite direction
they must pass on the right-hand side. In
passing a vehicle going in the same direc
tion they must pass on the left-hand side
Sec. 5. Wheelmen may ride their ma
j chines on the walk running along the
south of the speed track. Also on the
walk commencing at or near Thirty-fifth
avenue, i! extended on the north drive
and terminating at about Forty-eighth
avenue, if extended.
Sec. 6. On days on which music is played
in Concert valley the speed of bicycles on
the main drive, between Stanyah street
| and Sixth avenue, shall be limited to six
miles an hour.
Sec. 7. All persons are prohibited from
; riding, pushing or driving any bicycle, tri-
I cycle or similar vehicle along the main
i drive where the bicycle path parallels said
! drive.
Sec. 8. All persons are prohibited from
traveling on the road known as the bicycle
path, except they be riding or leading a
bicycle, tricycle or similar vehicle.
Sec. 9. All person are prohibited from
riding or driving any bicycle or similar ve
hicle at a greater rate of speed than ten
miles per hour or one mile in six minutes.
Sec. 10. Any person who shall violate
any of the provisions of this ordinance
shall )ip doemcd guilty of a misdemeanor
and upon conviction shall be punished by
a line of not less than five dollars nor
more than five hundred dollars or by im
prisonment in the County Jail for not less
than five days nor more than six months.
Among other business at the meeting
of the commissioners the resignation of
Irving' M. Scott, heretofore announced
exclusively in The Call, was accepted.
It was also decided to build a number
of sheds at the end of the speed track to
accommodate horsemen who drive valu
able animals over that course. Heretofore
horsefl were liable to t;iko cold after
sprinting over the track, but the innova
tion will obviate the danger, as it will
allow owners to blanket and house their
animals until cooled off.
A bridle path will soon be added to the
many excellent improvements in the Park.
Plans and specifications were examined
by the commissioners and it is probable
that orders will be issued in a few days
for the beginning of work on the path.
COOKS INLET HAS
NO CUSTOM-HOUSE.
VESSELS CANNOT GET CLEAR
ANCE FROM THAT PORT.
The Only Revenue Official Left That
Place Last Fall and No One
Knows When He Will
Return.
According to the story told by the map
ter of the schooner Thomas S. Negus the
Treasury Department would do well to
appoint a Customs Inspector at some
known or easily discoverable point in
Cooks Inlet, Alaska. There Is a rumor in
the department at Washing-ton that there
is such an inspector in the inlet, but the
rumor is unfounded.
The Negus arrived from Cooks Inlet yes
terday, after taking some mining outfits
to that point. Before leaving the schooner
was unable to clear through the Custom
house at that place for the reason that no
Custom-house could be found, and while
there were plenty of seals there there was
no officer to put them on paper or even on
board. The Inlet is a very large body of
water, with a very long seaboard with nu
merous bays and Inlets. On March 31 the
Treasury Department Issued a circular
stating that the location of the Custom
house at the Inlet was Saldonia. This cir
cular was not received at the San Fran
cisco Custom-house until after the vessel
left for Alaska and the captain oTthe N>-
Kus, of course, was not advised of it. The
schooner made her first call at Coal Bay,
formerly known as Kashomaok Hay. Sal
donia is supposed to be upon this bay, but
the master was unable to find from any
one there that there ever had bern a Cus
toms otfioer at that point. He next called
at Staritchkof. where he found only na
tives, who could Impart no information of

Customs officers. The next call was at Ku
silof, at which place the superintendent of
the cannery informed the skipper that
there was a Customs official at Tuyonic.
The skipper, not willing to leave Alaska
without clearance papers, sailed to Tuyo
nic, where he found that a Customs In
spector had bcon stationed, but that that
officer had left for Seattle in the fall of
1897. and that he had not returned.
The Negus landed her passengers at Tu
yonic, and the master of the schooner
Rattler informed him that there was no
Customs Inspector at Salfornia, the Rat
tler having just left that port. There is
no Salfornia in Alaska, and the Rattler's
skipper no doubt meant Saldonia.
Under the circumstances Collector Jack
son exonerated the master of the Negus
from all bin me for having left Alaska
without clearance papers. The Collector
will call the attention of the Secretary of
the Treasury to the necessity for a Cus
toms Inspector in the frozen north.
TWO NEW PRIESTS.
Ordinations to Be Held To-Day by
the Bishop of California.
The Trinity ordinations, which last year
were held at San Mateo, will occur this
morning at the Cathedral Mission of the
Good Samaritan, and Rev. Edward Mor
gan, who for a year has acted as assist
ant rector of the mission, and Rev. David
Charles Gardner, assistant of St. Ste
phen's Church, will be advanced to the
priesthood. The Bishop of California will
officiate, and Rev. Messrs. Warfleld and
Kip will be the presenters. A large num
ber of Episcopal clergy will assist. The
sermon will be preached by the Rev.
George Wallace of San Mateo. Rev. Mr.
Morgan will soon leave for work in an
Eastern diocese. He has greatly endeared
himself to his parish by his faithful work
among the sick poor.
Unpermitted Cargo.
Collector Jackson has been instructed by
the Secretary of the Treasury to deny the
request to allow "unpermitted cargo" to
remain upon the wharf In case of any ves
sel which does not belong to a regularly
established steamship line.
While this order restricts the privilege
to vessels of regularly established steam
ship lines it is not held to prevent grant
ing the privilege to the Southern Pacific
Company for merchandise arriving in
transit, as the cars belong to a regular
line and are considered vessels when
bringing "In transit" poods.
Want War Bonds.
The people of this city are just begin
ning to awaken to the fact that they have
an excellent opportunity to invest their
spare money in safe securities, the new 3
per cent war bonds that the Government
has recently authorized. Books for sub
scriptions were opened on Monday morn
ing at the First National Bank "and by
the time of closing about $125,000 had been
subscribed. While many were for the
larger denominations. $500 and $1000 still
many availed themselves of the chance to
invest in bonds of from $25 up. Yester
day the rush commenced in earnest and
when the bank closed the amount sub
scribed was something over $400,000. So
far the largest single subscription was for
$50,000. While the bonds are being bar
gained for at par value it is very likely
that as soon as the complement is filled
they will be at a premium. The total
issue will be $300,000,000.
May Have Attempted Suicide.
Rose Williams, who gives vapor baths
for a living at 1217 Market street, was
taken from her room by Sergeant Dono
van and Officer O'Keefe and sent to the
Receiving Hospital for treatment. The
woman was in a stupor, and showed signs
of poisoning. The usual remedies were
applied by Dr. Frank E. Fitzgibbon, and
she was put to bed. Up to a late hour
she had not gained consciousness. A
further search of her room disclosed a
letter addressed to Fred Burger, an em
ploye in the Baldwin Grotto. In it she
reproached him for his cruelty in desert
ing her, and ended with words of forgive
ness. It is not thought likely that she
will recover, as she is weak and emaci
ated.
Last Night's Fires.
While some employes were bottling
liquors at the establishment of Samuel
Brothers, 132-134 First street, last even
ing sealing wax used in the operation
caught fire and was the origin of a $200
blaze.
At 8:30 o'clock an alarm was turned in
from box 356 for a fire on the Lombnrd
street wharf. It Is supposed that c. fish
erman left a lighted cigarette on one of
the stringers and the wood became ig
nited. The loss was nominal.
